Tonawanda v. Ayler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


115 A.D.2d 940; 497 N.Y.S.2d 781; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 55298



December 20, 1985 

 

662 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Gribetz v. Evans


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


113 A.D.2d 376; 496 N.Y.S.2d 465; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 52933



December 9, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment (one paper) of the Supreme Court, Rockland County, dated March 7, 1984, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of the order and judgment of the Supreme Court, Rockland County (New York), which granted a motion by respondents, administrative judge and the state, for summary judgment and declared that respondents were not obligated under N.Y. Jud. Law § 39 to reimburse the county for the cost of all court security services, and that § 39 did not violate the taxpayer's constitutional right to equal protection of the laws.


OVERVIEW: A taxpayer brought an action against the administrative judge of the state court system and the state for a declaratory judgment regarding the obligation of the state under N.Y. Jud. Law § 39 to fully reimburse a county for all costs incurred by the county for extraordinary security measures taken in connection with certain local court cases. The taxpayer also claimed that the statute violated his constitutional right to equal protection of the laws because other political subdivisions of the state received total reimbursement for such security costs. The administrative judge and the state moved for summary judgment, which the lower court granted. The taxpayer sought review. On review, the court examined N.Y. Jud. Law § 39 and found that it did not mandate full reimbursement to localities for security costs. It merely created a technical change in the means of reimbursement. The court rejected the taxpayer's equal protection argument because the state had a rational basis for reimbursing security costs in different areas of the state in varying proportions. The court found no issue of fact in dispute to require a trial and held that summary judgment was appropriately granted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and judgment of the lower court in favor of the administrative judge and the state.


HEADNOTES

Counties -- Reimbursement for Judicial Security Measures

 Judiciary Law § 39 does not obligate the State of New York to fully reimburse the County of Rockland for all costs incurred by the county for security measures taken in connection with several criminal actions arising out of a well-publicized 1981 incident in which several members of a revolutionary terrorist group stole $ 1,600,000 from an armored truck and killed a security guard and two police officers, the County Sheriff having implemented extraordinary security measures to protect the general [***2] public and those individuals who were directly involved in the case from possible terrorist retaliation; the statute provides that reimbursement for court security costs is subject to the standard budgetary appropriation process, to be increased or decreased by available funds, and contains the concept of limited reimbursement of these costs in that it states that "[political] subdivisions * * * shall be entitled to reimbursement by the State within the amounts appropriated". Further, the Legislature's decision to differentiate between the large downstate urban counties and the other counties in the State in its statutory scheme for funding the respective court security needs of the Unified Court System is supported by a rational basis, and there is, accordingly, no equal protection violation.

 

663 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


ADT Co. v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


113 A.D.2d 140; 495 N.Y.S.2d 274; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 52341



November 27, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer appealed an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which dismissed the taxpayer's request to annul a decision of respondent, the New York State Tax Commission (commission), that assessed the taxpayer with sales taxes on annual and advance service charges for alarm systems.


OVERVIEW: The lower court dismissed the taxpayer's challenge to the commission's assessment for a sales tax deficiency on the taxpayer's annual charges for maintenance of local home alarm systems and on advance service charges for local and central alarm systems, which were tangible personal property. On appeal, the taxpayer asserted that the retroactive assessment of a sales tax deficiency was unconstitutional and inequitable, and that its annual and advance charges were exempt from sales tax pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 1105(c)(3)(iii). The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that the exemption of § 1105(c)(3)(iii) for additions or capital improvements to real property did not apply because the taxpayer retained the right to remove the alarm system. Likewise, the advance services charges for installation of local and central systems were taxable installation charges, given that the taxpayer retained ownership of the alarm systems. The retroactive assessment was constitutional because the taxpayer did not prove that he relied on a prior commission interpretation of § 1105(c)(3)(iii) and the commission's change of interpretation was not palpably unjust.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, which dismissed the taxpayer's petition to annul the commission's determination that the taxpayer owed sales taxes on annual and service charges for its alarm systems.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against Body or Officer -- When Remedy Appropriate

1. A CPLR article 78 proceeding is a proper vehicle for challenging the constitutionality and applicability to petitioner of Tax Law § 1105.

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Burglar and Fire Alarm Systems

2. Inasmuch as petitioner, who installs, sells, operates and maintains burglar and fire alarm systems, retained the legal right to remove the alarm system equipment from the premises of its subscribers, the systems did not constitute real property within the exception to the sales tax provided in Tax Law § 1105 (c) (3) (iii) and, therefore, the determination of respondent New York State Tax Commission that petitioner's revenues from annual local alarm services were subject to said sales tax will not be annulled.

Taxation  [***2] -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Burglar and Fire Alarm Systems

3. Advance service charges levied by petitioner, who installs, sells, operates and maintains burglar and fire alarm systems, for installing both its local alarm and central station systems are taxable installation changes pursuant to Tax Law § 1105 (c) (3); even if a subscriber exercised his right to retain certain equipment under the service contracts, all petitioner would be deprived of is the opportunity to recover material that would cost more to remove than to leave in place; moreover, under petitioner's various central station contracts, it retained ownership of the equipment installed; thus, the property installed does not constitute an addition or capital improvement to real property such as would be excepted from the tax by section 1105 (c) (3) (iii).

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Burglar and Fire Alarm Systems -- Retroactive Assessment

4. Retroactive assessment of a sales tax deficiency against petitioner pursuant to Tax Law § 1105 (c) (3) for annual charges for local alarm services and advance service charges for both local and central station systems is not unconstitutional and inequitable on [***3] the ground that the prevailing interpretation of the statute throughout the audit period favored classifying petitioner's service as excepted from sales tax; the interpretation relied upon by petitioner applied to alarm systems considered to be appurtenant to telegraph lines, which are not utilized for petitioner's local alarm systems; further, there is no indication that petitioner actually relied on that interpretation as a basis for failing to carry out its statutory duty to collect the sales tax during the audit period; moreover, a retroactive change in respondent's interpretation is not invalid per se since it has not been categorically demonstrated that respondent New York State Tax Commission's interpretation of the Tax Law is palpably unjust.

 

664 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Laiosa v. Camelot AMC/Jeep, Ltd.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


113 A.D.2d 145; 495 N.Y.S.2d 285; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 52342



November 27, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as dismissed the first cause of action against defendants American Motors, Inc., and American Motors Sales Corporation, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff car buyers sought review of an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which denied their motion for summary judgment against defendants, car dealer, car manufacturer, and car corporation, in the car buyers' action for breach of express and implied warranties, and under N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 198-a and the Magnuson-Moss Warranty Act (Act), 15 U.S.C.S. §§ 2301-2312.


OVERVIEW: The car buyers commenced an action alleging causes of action under N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 198-a and the Act, and for breach of both express and implied warranties. The car buyers' then sought summary judgment on the two statutory causes of action. The trial court denied the motion with respect to the claim under the Act. The trial court also dismissed all claims against the car dealer and granted summary judgment to the car manufacturer and car corporation on the cause of action under § 198-a. The car buyers sought review of these decisions. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision that denied the car buyers' motion as it related to the claim under the Act. The court found that although the Act was applicable to the case, a question of fact existed as to whether a reasonable number of repair attempts had already been made on the vehicle in question. The court reversed the trial court's order that granted summary judgment to the car manufacturer and car corporation on the claim under § 198-a. The car buyers were entitled to statutory protection because they were entitled by the terms of their warranty to enforce that warranty as of the statute's effective date.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision that denied the car buyers' motion for summary judgment on their claim under the Act. The court reversed the trial court's order that granted summary judgment to the car manufacturer and car corporation on the car buyers' claim under the New York General Business Law.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Warranties -- Action under Federal Magnuson-Moss Warranty Act

1. A motion by plaintiffs for summary judgment on their cause of action under the Magnuson-Moss Warranty Act (15 USC §§ 2301-2312) was properly denied where there exists a question of fact as to whether a reasonable number of repair attempts have already been made by defendant warrantor to remedy the defects or malfunctions in plaintiffs' automobile; what constitutes a reasonable number of repair attempts has not been defined by statute or regulation.

Motor Vehicles -- Warranties -- Applicability of State Automobile Lemon Law -- Substantial Impairment of Value

2. General Business Law § 198-a, the automobile lemon law, which provides that a consumer to whom the benefits [***2] of the statute extend includes one who is entitled by the terms of a warranty to enforce the obligations of the warranty, applies to plaintiffs who had purchased their car nearly 10 months before the law's effective date since they were clearly entitled by the terms of their warranty to enforce that warranty as of the statute's effective date; however, the presence of issues of fact, such as whether the alleged defect substantially impairs the car's value (General Business Law § 198-a [c]), precludes the granting of summary judgment to plaintiffs on their cause of action under section 198-a.

Constitutional Law -- Impairment of Contract -- Automobile Lemon Law

3. Application of General Business Law § 198-a, the automobile lemon law, to plaintiffs' automobile purchase will not result in an unconstitutional impairment of contractual obligations (US Const, art I, § 10); since the warranty on the car purchased by plaintiffs was a full warranty already subject to the provisions of the Federal Magnuson-Moss Warranty Act, application of the State statute, which provides a remedy similar to that provided by the Federal act, will not result in a substantial impairment of any [***3] party's contractual rights.

 

665 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Knapp


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


113 A.D.2d 154; 495 N.Y.S.2d 985; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 52344



November 21, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision from the Otsego County Court (New York), which entered judgment convicting defendant of second-degree manslaughter. Defendant raised issues concerning the failure to change venue, improper void dire, the holding of the trial in a church hall, the admittance of evidence, the denial of a motion to suppress, the sufficiency of the evidence, a jury charge, and the sentencing.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's original conviction was reversed. In the second trial, defendant was acquitted of reckless murder, but he was convicted of the lesser-included offense. On appeal, the court agreed with the trial court in full. While a defendant had a constitutional right to a fair trial, the fact that the jury was aware of the crime prior to trial did not make the trial unfair. The presence of the media in voir dire did not prejudice defendant. The holding of the trial in a church hall, while not ideal, did not render the trial unfair. Evidence that defendant struck the victim was admissible. The state did not violate the appellate court's finding that a motion to suppress should have been granted in the first trial. The evidence the state used was not the subject of the first suppression motion. Also, the evidence was too attenuated to be the fruit of the poisonous tree. Evidence supported the conviction. The evidence did not support a finding that defendant should have been convicted of criminal negligent homicide or reckless endangerment instead. The jury was properly charged. The sentence was properly enhanced for a prior record.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for second-degree manslaughter was affirmed. Defendant's right to a fair trial was preserved. The jury was properly chosen and instructed. The evidence was sufficient to convict. The sentence was properly enhanced and was not excessive.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Appeal -- Law of the Case

1. On appeal to the Appellate Division from a judgment convicting defendant of manslaughter in the second degree, allegations of error as to the denial by the Appellate Division of two pretrial motions to change venue are not properly raised since the decisions denying the motions are the law of the case; however, the issue will be treated as a contention that defendant was denied his constitutional right to a fair trial by an impartial jury by reason of extensive pretrial publicity.

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Publicity

2. Defendant's conviction of manslaughter in the second degree, following a second trial after a prior conviction of reckless murder was reversed by the Court of Appeals, was not obtained in a trial atmosphere that had been utterly corrupted by press coverage; although the publicity surrounding the initial trial, the appellate proceedings and the retrial was extensive and involved confessions and other evidence [***2] which the Court of Appeals ordered suppressed upon the second trial, the voir dire examination of the jurors for the second trial was extensive and thorough and provided an opportunity to eliminate jurors who were aware of the suppressed evidence or who were otherwise unable to render an impartial verdict.

Crimes -- Voir Dire -- Exclusion of Media and Public

3. The voir dire examination of jurors for defendant's second trial on a charge of reckless murder, following reversal by the Court of Appeals of his prior conviction on that charge, was not improperly conducted on the ground that the media and public were not excluded while jurors were questioned regarding publicity as to prejudicial matters thereby resulting in other potential jurors being informed of the prejudicial matters; the media was already aware of the prejudicial matters, had been reporting them throughout the history of the litigation and was free to continue to report them and, thus, the presence of the media did not prejudice defendant.

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Trial Held in Church Hall

4. Defendant was not denied a fair and public trial because, due to courthouse renovations, his trial was [***3] held in the church hall of a Roman Catholic church; while the church hall was a private building, public access was not restricted during the trial; further, although the church hall may not have been an ideal place for the trial, the religious environment was not so pervasive as to deny defendant a fair trial.

Crimes -- Evidence -- Admissibility of Evidence Relevant to Prior Acquittal

5. At the second trial of defendant on a charge of reckless murder, following his prior acquittal on an intentional murder charge and reversal by the Court of Appeals of his prior conviction of reckless murder, evidence that defendant struck the victim was properly admitted; although the principle of double jeopardy prohibited retrial of the intentional murder charge, the mere fact that evidence was relevant to the intentional murder count does not necessarily mean that it was inadmissible at the second trial; evidence that defendant struck the victim also bore some relevance to the reckless murder charge, particularly with regard to defendant's awareness that the victim was seriously injured; further, such evidence is relevant in establishing that defendant had a duty to aid the victim,  [***4] in response to defendant's argument that he was not required to be a good Samaritan.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Matters Reviewable

6. Allegations by defendant of error as to statements made by the prosecution during summation were not preserved for review where defendant failed to make timely objections or requests for corrective instructions; moreover, the comments do not warrant reversal as an exercise of discretion in the interest of justice ( CPL 470.15 [6] [a]).

Crimes -- Evidence -- Suppression at First Trial -- Admissibility at Second Trial

7. Evidence consisting of the victim's body, police officers' testimony regarding what they saw at the time of the arrest and an inculpatory spontaneous utterance by defendant, admitted at the second trial of defendant on a charge of reckless murder, did not violate the prior decision of the Court of Appeals suppressing statements made by defendant to a police informant, a written confession and incriminating items discovered in defendant's car by police pursuant to a search warrant, and will not be suppressed on the ground that they were fruits of the unlawfully obtained statements made to the informant; inasmuch as the [***5] decision of the Court of Appeals did not involve any new or novel interpretation of law which would justify the failure to seek suppression of all the evidence at issue prior to the first trial, it was limited to those items of evidence defendant had timely sought to suppress, and defendant did not seek to suppress the victim's body or the observations of the arresting officer until just before the second trial. Moreover, even if defendant's contention had been properly raised, it would be rejected on the merits since the defendant's independent act in consciously choosing to move the victim's body for burial at the informant's logging site, which was not planned by the informant or police, along with the fact that, prior to the illegality, the police had strong reason to investigate defendant, acted to dissipate the taint of the unlawfully obtained statements.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Prima Facie Case

8. Whether or not the People failed to present a prima facie case to support the reckless murder charge upon which defendant was tried is irrelevant on defendant's appeal from the judgment convicting him of the lesser-included offense of second degree manslaughter.

Crimes  [***6] -- Manslaughter -- Manslaughter in Second Degree

9. In the prosecution of defendant charged with reckless murder, the victim having died from complications caused by a fractured skull, where the People sought to establish the charge by proving that the victim was knocked unconscious, either by being stricken by defendant or by falling or jumping from defendant's moving car, and that defendant failed to transport her for medical care, a prima facie case of the lesser included offense of second degree manslaughter, which occurs when one recklessly causes the death of another (Penal Law § 125.15 [1]), was established; the jury was free to credit the testimony of the People's expert that the victim did not die instantly and that prompt medical attention could have saved her; there is also evidence that defendant affirmatively moved her to a more isolated location, thus preventing any chance that a passing motorist could have aided her; further, there is testimony of a former jail inmate that defendant had told him that he struck the girl and placed her in the woods and, even if the victim voluntarily leapt from the car, it cannot be said that defendant had no duty to take some sort [***7] of action; defendant's conduct in leaving the unconscious girl abandoned in an isolated location on a winter night was reckless regardless of whether he knew the victim's skull was fractured.

Crimes -- Lesser Included Offense -- Reasonable View of Evidence

10. In the prosecution of defendant charged with reckless murder arising out of the death of the victim as a result of complications caused by a fractured skull where the People sought to establish the charge by proving that the victim was knocked unconscious, either by being stricken by defendant or by falling or jumping from defendant's moving car, and that defendant failed to transport her for medical care, there is no reasonable view of the evidence upon which it could be found that defendant committed the lesser included offenses of criminally negligent homicide, first degree reckless endangerment and second degree reckless endangerment and, therefore, said offenses need not have been charged; it is unconceivable that a person could fail to perceive the risk of leaving an unconscious person unattended in an isolated location on a winter night and, thus, under no reasonable view was defendant's conduct criminally [***8] negligent; moreover, to have submitted reckless endangerment to the jury would have required it to speculate on the victim's chance of survival and, thus, there is no reasonable view which would support a charge of reckless endangerment.

Crimes -- Instructions

11. In the prosecution of defendant charged with reckless murder where the court charged the jury as to the lesser included offense of second degree manslaughter and the jury asked twice how the charge would be affected if the victim had died instantly or if defendant so believed, County Court's answers that the charge presupposed that the victim was alive after she sustained the injuries and that failure to obtain medical attention caused her death were not misleading; from the tenor of the questions, it is clear that the jury was cognizant of this crucial factual issue in the case; in no way could the answers be misinterpreted to indicate that the jury had to assume that the victim was alive.

Crimes -- Instructions -- Requests to Charge Jury

12. It was not error to refuse to grant defendant's special requests to charge which were either incorrect, confusing or sufficiently covered by County Court's instructions.

 [***9] Crimes -- Sentence -- Persistent Felony Offender

13. The new sentence which takes the place of a revoked sentence of probation can properly form the basis for a predicate felony conviction; further, a reformatory sentence suffices as a sentence of imprisonment for the purposes of persistent felony offender sentencing; thus, where defendant's sentence of probation for a felony conviction of second degree forgery was revoked, and defendant was sentenced to a reformatory term of 0 to 4 years, the reformatory sentence can properly serve as the basis for his sentencing as a persistent felony offender; moreover, defendant's prior conviction was not unconstitutionally obtained.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Harsh and Excessive Sentence

14. The sentencing of defendant, who was convicted of second degree manslaughter arising out of the death of an 18-year-old girl after she was abandoned in an injured condition in a secluded area on a winter night by defendant, as a persistent felony offender and imposing the maximum sentence authorized of 25 years to life is neither harsh nor excessive considering the heinous nature of the crime and the obvious fact that defendant must be removed [***10] from society for the protection of its citizens; that the sentence imposed was the same as that imposed upon his subsequently reversed prior conviction of second degree murder based on the girl's death cannot be construed as a penalty for defendant having succeeded on appeal since defendant was not charged as a persistent felony offender after the first trial.

People v. Daniels


SUPREME COURT OF NEW YORK, APPELLATE DIVISION, SECOND DEPARTMENT


114 A.D.2d 968; 495 N.Y.S.2d 920; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 54011



November 18, 1985 

 

667 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Flowers v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


115 A.D.2d 226; 496 N.Y.S.2d 149; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 54472



November 15, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a correctional facility, sought review of a decision by the Supreme Court, Wyoming County (New York) that entered a judgment ordering that the facility return to appellee inmate, all moneys unconstitutionally recouped from his inmate account to provide for excess postage used by the inmate.


OVERVIEW: The recoupment was made pursuant to a department directive which instituted a program providing inmates free postage for five letters per week and deducting from an inmate's account, the cost of excess postage. In holding that the deduction was unconstitutional, the lower court compared the act to the garnishment of a judgment debtor's wages, which was deemed unconstitutional. The court held that there was no equal protection violation because there was a rational distinction between the recoupment of money from an inmate's account and a garnishment against the wages of a judgment debtor. The court held that compelling inmates to reimburse the state for the cost of excess postage did not deprive them of their property without due process. The court reasoned that unlike a judgement debtor, no judgment was docketed against the inmate and there was no provision for recoupment after he was released from prison.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and ordered restoration of the inmate's money.

Hospital Asso. of New York State v. Axelrod


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


113 A.D.2d 9; 494 N.Y.S.2d 905; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 52045



October 29, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law, without costs, by declaring that section 19 of the Medical Malpractice Reform Act is not void and unenforceable because sections 20 and 21 thereof mandate that the hospitals be fully reimbursed for the cost of providing excess insurance coverage to physicians and dentists, and, as so modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hospital association sought review of a decision of Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which denied the association's motion for summary judgment against defendants, the State Department of Health and its commissioner, in the association's action challenging the Medical Malpractice Reform Act, 1985 N.Y. Laws ch. 294.


OVERVIEW: In an effort to reform New York's medical malpractice liability system, the Medical Malpractice Reform Act (Act), 1985 N.Y. Laws ch. 294, was passed. The association brought an action challenging § 19 of the Act, claiming that the general hospitals would be required to absorb without reimbursement the Medicare portion of their costs of obtaining the excess coverage required by it, and filed a motion for summary judgment against defendants, which the trial court denied. The association appealed, seeking a judgment declaring the invalidity of § 19 as implemented by defendants. The court modified the trial court's order by declaring that § 19 was not void and unenforceable because §§ 20 and 21 mandated that the hospitals be fully reimbursed for the cost of providing the excess coverage. The court found that the imposition on the hospitals of substantial costs in procuring the excess coverage sufficiently raised doubts as to the legislation's constitutionality and thus, the court declared that §§ 20 and 21 required full reimbursement to the hospitals, in an effort to more widely distribute the cost burden among the ultimate consumers of health services, as intended by the Act.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order of the trial court, on the law, and without costs, by declaring that the Medical Malpractice Reform Act was not void and unenforceable.


HEADNOTES

Declaratory Judgments -- When Remedy Appropriate -- Justiciable Controversy

1. An action in which plaintiff seeks a judgment declaring the invalidity of those portions of the Medical Malpractice Reform Act (L 1985, ch 294), which obligate general hospitals in the State to purchase certain excess insurance coverage on behalf of any physician or dentist having the requisite primary coverage who designates it as the hospital with whom the practitioner is primarily affiliated, is ripe for declaratory relief; defendants have refused to guarantee full reimbursement of the costs of obtaining the insurance for the first six months of the year for which the coverage is required [***2] and have advised that they will do nothing to make up for the certain loss of Medicare-related reimbursement for the second six months. Accordingly, the issue clearly represents a present, actual controversy which furnishes the necessary justiciability for seeking declaratory relief.

Hospitals and Asylums -- Malpractice -- Mandated Excess Insurance Coverage -- Reimbursement by Third-Party Payors

2. In order to sustain the constitutionality of the Medical Malpractice Reform Act (L 1985, ch 294), pursuant to which malpractice insurance carriers are mandated to offer excess coverage of $ 1 million/$ 3 million to physicians and dentists having primary coverage in those amounts and general hospitals are to purchase such excess coverage on behalf of any physician or dentist having the requisite primary coverage who designates it as the hospital with whom the practitioner is primarily affiliated, the statute is to be construed such that the full cost of the excess coverage is to be passed on to the State's principal third-party payors of hospital inpatient care charges through the Commissioner of Health's rate determination authority.

 

670 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Fossella v. Dinkins


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


110 A.D.2d 227; 494 N.Y.S.2d 878; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 50922



October 25, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Richmond County, dated October 23, 1985, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.

Cross appeal by petitioners dismissed, without costs or disbursements (see, Parochial Bus Sys. v Board of Educ., 60 NY2d 539).

Motion by appellants-respondents, brought on by order to show cause dated October 24, 1985, denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent board of elections (board) appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Richmond County (New York), which granted the application of petitioner individuals to invalidate a proposed local law amending New York City, N.Y., Charter § 67, and enjoined the board from placing the referendum on the ballot at the general election.


OVERVIEW: The board proposed to amend § 67 to prohibit municipal authorities from consenting to the use of city-owned property for the development of any military facility at which nuclear weapons were deployed. The lower court granted the individuals' petition to declare the proposed amendment unconstitutional and enjoined the board from placing the referendum on the ballot at the general election. On appeal, the court affirmed because the proposed amendment curtailed the power of the Board of Estimate to approve dispositions of city property or expenditures to facilitate the development of a military facility in which nuclear weapons were stored. The proposed amendment interfered with the federal government's exercise of the power to establish a military base and raise and maintain a navy and army under U.S. Const. art. I, § 8. The court held that the proposed amendment, by inhibiting the city from cooperating in the development of the project relating to a matter of national defense, unconstitutionally interfered with the federal government's plenary power to prepare for and wage war and provide a common defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the board's proposed amendment to the city charter was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Prohibition of Use of City Property for Military Facility

A proposed amendment to New York City Charter § 67 which would, among other things, prohibit local municipal authorities from consenting to the use of city owned property for the development of any military [***2] facility at which nuclear weapons are to be deployed is unconstitutional; any State or local law, including any provision of the New York City Charter, which would inhibit State or local officials from cooperating with the Federal authorities in respect to a matter of National defense would interfere with the exclusive and plenary power of the Federal Government to prepare for, and wage war, and to provide for a common defense and would, therefore, violate the United States Constitution. Furthermore, to the extent that the proposed amendment to New York City Charter § 67 would prohibit the Board of Estimate from approving "any sale, lease, exchange or other disposition * * * of any city owned property, or street" to the Federal Government "to facilitate the development of any military facility, any component of which is designed to carry or store nuclear weapons", it is violative of State Law § 50 (2).

Fossella v. Dinkins

Mun. Home Rule Law § 37 for an amendment to be placed on the ballot pertaining the expenditure of city funds for military nuclear use.

The protestors filed a motion to intervene in the proceeding to invalidate their petition, and they also filed a motion to dismiss the petitioner's action to invalidate the protestor's petition. 673 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Megalopolis Property Asso. v. Buvron


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


110 A.D.2d 232; 494 N.Y.S.2d 14; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 50923



September 30, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Upon appeal by permission, order of the Appellate Term for the Second and Eleventh Judicial Districts, dated June 8, 1984, reversed, on the law, with costs, and judgment of the Civil Court, Queens County, dated November 9, 1983, which dismissed the proceeding, reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant tenant sought review of an order of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department, Second and Eleventh Judicial Districts (New York), which reversed a judgment from the lower court that had dismissed respondent landlord's petition in a holdover summary proceeding where respondent sought to evict appellant.


OVERVIEW: Respondent landlord commenced a holdover summary proceeding seeking to evict appellant tenant on the ground that appellant had failed to cure his violation of the no-pet clause in the parties' lease. Before the proceeding came on for trial, the New York City, N.Y., Administrative Code § D26-10.10 (1983) was signed into law. The lower court dismissed the proceeding on the ground that the new pet law, § D26-10.10 applied retroactively to protect appellant because he had maintained his pet openly and notoriously for the requisite three-month period. Respondent sought review and the appellate court reversed the lower court's judgment and reinstated the proceeding. Appellant sought review and the court reversed the order of the appellate court and reinstated the judgment of the lower court that had dismissed the case. The court found that the law protected a tenant whose landlord had knowledge of the harboring of a pet for more than three months prior to the commencement of an action or proceeding to enforce the lease. The court held that to evict appellant after six years of openly living with a dog in the premises, would have completely frustrated the intentions of § D26-10.10.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate court and reinstated the judgment of the lower court that had dismissed the case. The court held that the new pet law was applicable and found a dismissal in favor of appellant tenant was warranted.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Harboring Animals in Leased Premises -- Retroactive Application of Pet Law

Administrative Code of City of New York § D26-10.10, effective October 26, 1983, which states that a lease provision prohibiting the harboring of household pets is deemed waived where a tenant openly and notoriously has harbored a household pet for a period of three months or more following taking possession of the apartment and the owner or his agent has knowledge of this fact but fails within the three-month period to commence [***2] a summary proceeding or action to enforce the lease provision, is applicable to an eviction proceeding commenced prior to the law's enactment; the law applies to all existing leases, and while it does not specifically indicate that it is to be applied to pending eviction proceedings, it protects a tenant whose landlord had knowledge of the harboring of a pet for more than three months prior to the commencement of an action or proceeding to enforce the lease. Giving effect to the new law in such a case does not unconstitutionally impair a landlord's preexisting contract rights since the right to contract is not absolute and may be interfered with by subsequent legislation or regulation so long as it is reasonably necessary to further an important public purpose and the measures taken that impair the contract are reasonable and appropriate to effectuate that purpose.

Skinner v. Sylvan Beach


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


113 A.D.2d 1000; 494 N.Y.S.2d 580; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 52616



September 26, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners and defendant Village of Sylvan Beach, N.Y., appealed an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Oneida County (New York), which granted in part and denied in part plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment in their declaratory judgment action seeking to invalidate Village of Sylvan Beach, N.Y., Local Laws, 1977, No. 1, art. X, on the ground that the local law apportioning sewer system costs was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs asserted that the local law failed to assess a tax upon vacant land and that it imposed a tax that was measured solely by the amount of use that a particular property owner made of the sewer system. On appeal by both parties, the court concluded that the special term improperly denied plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment on two of the causes of action. The court declared that the local law was invalid as unconstitutional and contrary to law. The court held that the local law was invalid because it imposed no charge on the 300-some vacant properties; the local law therefore placed an unconstitutional burden on the improved properties that were taxed. The court pointed to the well settled law that all properties within an area to be served by an improvement were, to some extent, actually benefitted and were therefore assessable. The court also held that the local law was invalid because the charges were essentially use-based and were not proportionate to or measured by the benefit accruing to the property from being included within the area served by the sewer system. For example, a home on a small lot would have paid the same charge as a home located on several acres.


OUTCOME: The court unanimously modified, on the law, the order by declaring the law local unconstitutional. The court affirmed in part, agreeing with the special term's determination to dismiss the various enumerated affirmative defenses; however, the court dismissed any remaining defenses except as to the fourth affirmative defense. The court dismissed plaintiffs' appeal of other aspects of the special term's order.

People v. Ruffino


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


110 A.D.2d 198; 494 N.Y.S.2d 8; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 50919



September 23, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Queens County, rendered October 4, 1983, as amended October 19, 1983, reversed, on the law, and new trial ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Supreme Court, Queens County (New York), which convicted him of robbery in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with first-degree robbery by a single complaining witness after a photograph lineup followed by lineup identification. Defendant's effort to suppress the identification testimony was unsuccessful. The trial court convicted defendant after it determined that evidence relating to identification had been resolved in a pretrial hearing and would not be permitted in the trial. On appeal, defendant asserted that the trial court erred in its refusal to permit the jury to consider the fairness of the lineup in determining whether defendant's identity had been proven beyond a reasonable doubt. The court reversed and remanded for a new trial because it determined that after identification testimony was determined to be admissible, it was still a matter to be determined by the trier of fact whether the evidence was sufficient to establish guilt beyond a reasonable doubt and defendant should have been allowed to introduce evidence to establish that the pretrial procedure was itself so suggestive as to create a reasonable doubt regarding the accuracy of that identification and of any subsequent in-court identification..


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order convicting defendant of first-degree robbery and remanded for a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Identification of Defendant

1. Although a motion to suppress identification testimony made pursuant to CPL article 710 is the exclusive method of challenging the admissibility of such evidence ( CPL 710.70 [3]), a defendant is also entitled to have the jury consider the fairness of pretrial identification procedures in determining whether testimony identifying him as the perpetrator of a crime is sufficient to establish guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. Accordingly, where a suppression court had determined that a pretrial lineup had not been unduly suggestive, the trial court erred in striking certain testimony proffered by the defense regarding the composition and conduct of that lineup, and advising the jury that there had already been a judicial determination [***2] of the fairness of the lineup, and a new trial is ordered.

Crimes -- Identification of Defendant

2. Notwithstanding the suppression court's determination that the photographic procedures utilized were unduly suggestive, the complainant's incourt identification, and evidence of the lineup identification, were properly admitted at the trial of a defendant convicted of robbery in the first degree; the evidence of the complainant's opportunity to view defendant during the perpetration of the crime was sufficient to establish an independent basis, and the lineup identification, which was made approximately three and one-half months after the suggestive photographic procedures, was sufficiently attenuated and was not itself suggestive.

People v. Cheeks


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


113 A.D.2d 974; 493 N.Y.S.2d 518; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 52596



September 12, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the County Court of Chemung County (New York), which convicted him of the crime of promoting prison contraband in the first degree, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 205.25(2). Defendant was sentenced as a second felony offender, under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a state prison inmate, broke open a disposable razor that had been given to him in a prison-issued shaving kit; he was charged when he was found hiding the razor blade, that he had extracted from the razor, in his mouth. After his conviction, defendant sought review and asserted that the evidence was insufficient to support his conviction and that the second felony offender statute under which he was sentenced was unconstitutional. In affirming the judgment, the court rejected the constitutionality argument, noting that the constitutionality of § 70.06 had long been upheld.A prison inmate could be found guilty of promoting prison contraband in the first degree if he knowingly and unlawfully made, obtained, or possessed any dangerous contraband, which included any item that an inmate was prohibited from possessing or contraband that was capable of being used to endanger the safety of others in the facility, N.Y. Penal Law §§ 205.25(2), 205.00(3), 205.055(4).The fact that the razor had been supplied to the inmate by prison authorities did not disqualify the item as contraband when the inmate altered the item to transform it into something that could be used as a weapon.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction.

In re Sarah K.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


110 A.D.2d 18; 492 N.Y.S.2d 957; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 49305



August 19, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Appeal and cross appeal from a nondispositional order of the Family Court, Suffolk County, dated September 24, 1984, dismissed, without costs or disbursement.

Order of the same court, dated November 7, 1984, reversed, without costs or disbursements, nondispositional order dated September 24, 1984, vacated, it is declared that the judicial and nonjudicial consents to adoption signed by the natural parents were void ab initio, adoption petition denied, and matter remitted to the Family Court, Suffolk County, for the entry of an order in its discretion providing for the transfer of custody of the infant from the adoptive to the natural parents as expeditiously as possible.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant natural parents sought review of a judgment of the Suffolk County Family Court (New York) that refused to give effect to their notice of revocation of consent to adoption and that granted the adoption petition.


OVERVIEW: After the natural parents gave birth to a child with Down's Syndrome, they immediately arranged for her adoption. The natural parents signed both a judicial and an extrajudicial consent form. Pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 115-b, the extrajudicial consent form did not become irrevocable until 30 days after adoption proceedings commenced, and before that time consent could be invoked by written notice. The judicial consent form was operative and irrevocable immediately upon execution. A couple days before the adoption hearing was scheduled, the natural parents forwarded a revocation of consent form to the court. The trial court rejected their revocation and granted the adoption petition. The natural parents appealed, and the court reversed. The court held that § 115-b was unconstitutional. The statute failed to notify the natural parents of all the legal consequences of revoking their consent. Rather, the extrajudicial consent form was deceptive in conveying that consent could be wholly extinguished within 30 days of the initiation of adoption proceedings. Because § 115-b was unconstitutional, the natural parents' giving of consent pursuant to that statute was invalid.


OUTCOME: The judgment that refused to give effect to the natural parents' revocation of consent to adoption and granted the adoption petition was reversed. The appeal and cross appeal were dismissed. The extrajudicial consent adoption law was found unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Adoption  [***2] -- Consent -- Revocation

In an adoption proceeding a dispositional order, made after a hearing to determine whether the best interests of the child required that a notice of revocation of an extrajudicial consent to the adoption filed by the natural parents be given effect and thereupon the infant be returned to her natural parents or that the adoption petition be granted, which order refused to give effect to said notice and, in effect, granted the petition, is reversed, a nondispositional order which deemed said notice of revocation to have been timely given and directed said best interests hearing is vacated, the judicial and nonjudicial consents to adoption signed by the natural parents are declared void ab initio, the adoption petition is denied and the matter remitted to Family Court for the transfer of the custody of the infant from the adoptive parents to the natural parents as expeditiously as possible.

Propp v. Propp


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


112 A.D.2d 868; 493 N.Y.S.2d 147; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 52093



August 15, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:

OVERVIEW: One day before the parties were married, they executed a prenuptial agreement providing that upon entry of a decree of divorce proceeding on any ground other than the wife's actionable misconduct as construed under N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 236, the husband would be obligated to make payments to the wife in amounts determined in part by the parties' income. The husband commenced an action for divorce based on cruel and inhuman treatment and asked that alimony be fixed in accordance with the agreement. The wife asserted as a defense that the agreement was unenforceable and void as being in violation of former N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law § 5-311. Thus, the husband's assets and income were relevant to a determination as to whether, and to what extent, the prenuptial agreement was enforceable.

679 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Paluska


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


109 A.D.2d 389; 491 N.Y.S.2d 999; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 48248



July 18, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, defendant's plea of guilty vacated, and matter remitted to Supreme Court for further proceedings not inconsistent herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court in Chemung County (New York), convicting defendant, upon his plea of guilty, of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Police obtained an eavesdropping warrant and overheard telephone calls in which defendant agreed to sell lysergic acid diethylamide. No tapes were sealed until nine days after the warrant expired. The lower court denied defendant's motion to suppress the tape recordings and statements, holding that the eavesdropping warrant and procedures were valid. Defendant pleaded guilty to criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree. On appeal, the court vacated the plea. The warrant provision that interception would commence as soon as practicable was not specific enough to change the general rule that the 30-day period commenced when the warrant was issued. Although the actual interception fell within the period, the tapes were not sealed until nine days after the warrant expired by statutory mandate. They were not sealed immediately upon expiration of the warrant as required.


OUTCOME: Defendant's guilty plea was vacated because the warrant provision that interception would commence as soon as practicable was not specific enough to change the rule that the 30-day period commenced when the warrant issued, and the tapes were not sealed immediately upon the warrant's expiration.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Eavesdropping Warrants -- 30-Day Durational Limit

1. A provision in an eavesdropping warrant that interception shall commence as soon as practicable is not specific enough to change the general rule that the 30-day outside durational limit on an eavesdropping warrant specified by CPL 700.10 (2) and 700.30 (7) begins on the date of issue unless another date is specified in the warrant by the issuing court; accordingly, where an eavesdropping warrant, issued on June 13, provided that eavesdropping would commence as soon as practicable and would continue for a period of 30 days from the date of the commencement of interception, the warrant should have expired by statutory mandate on July 12, notwithstanding that the police began interception on June 23; although the actual interception [***2] fell within the statutory 30-day period, thereby suggesting harmless error, the requirement that the eavesdropping tapes be sealed immediately upon expiration of the period of the warrant ( CPL 700.50) was violated because the tapes were not sealed until nine days after the warrant expired by statutory mandate and no explanation was offered by the prosecution for the nine-day delay.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Appellate Review

2. Where defendant moved in County Court, pursuant to CPL 710.20 (2), to determine the admissibility of statements intercepted by means of an eavesdropping warrant, but did not advance the specific theory he relied upon on his appeal from his judgment of conviction, he nonetheless sufficiently apprised County Court of his objections to the entire eavesdropping warrant, and thus the challenge to the warrant was presented below and thereby preserved for review on appeal.

 

680 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Suttell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


109 A.D.2d 249; 492 N.Y.S.2d 192; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 49748



July 12, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously reversed, on the law, and as a matter of discretion in the interest of justice, defendant's motion to suppress granted, and defendant remanded to Genesee County Court for further proceedings pursuant to CPL 410.70 (5).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Genesee County Court (New York) that convicted defendant, upon his plea of guilty, of criminal possession of a controlled substance in the fifth degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was on probation and, as a condition, his probation officer was allowed to search his home for drugs. Without obtaining a search warrant under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 410.50(3), the probation officer and police entered defendant's home and after a search they seized drugs. Defendant pleaded guilty to violation of probation and possession of a controlled substance. Defendant appealed alleging that the drugs should be suppressed since no search warrant was obtained prior to the search. The court agreed and reversed his conviction because, under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 410.50(3), absent exigent circumstances a warrant was required even for probationers. The court found no exigent circumstances; therefore, a search warrant was required. The court ruled that the defendant could have waived his constitutional and statutory rights, but there was no showing of waiver here.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was reversed and the motion to suppress was granted because defendant's probation officer failed to obtain a search warrant from a judge prior to the search of defendant's house and no exigent circumstances existed to alleviate the need for a search warrant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Search of Probationer without Court Order

A probation officer's failure to have obtained a prior judicial determination of reasonable cause is enough, standing alone, to invalidate the search of the person or residence of one on probation unless the circumstances with which the probation officer was confronted justify the failure to have done so. Accordingly, a quantity of marihuana seized from defendant probationer's bedroom by his probation officer should have been suppressed on the basis that the probation officer failed to obtain a search order pursuant to CPL 410.50 (3) before conducting the search, where the probation officer had ample opportunity to submit the matter to the court as required by the statute [***2] and there was no showing of exigency; an order which imposed conditions on defendant's probation and required defendant to permit the search of his person and premises for illegal drugs may not be equated with the court order required to conduct a valid search. Moreover, a statement signed by defendant in relation to the conditions of probation which asserts that defendant understands and will abide by said conditions does not constitute a waiver of his constitutional or statutory rights.

 

681 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Lilly v. Turecki


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


112 A.D.2d 788; 492 N.Y.S.2d 286; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 56032



July 12, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Order unanimously affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In their malpractice action against defendants, hospital and physician, plaintiff patients appealed from an order referring information that the patients sought in discovery for review to determine whether it was privileged information under N.Y. Educ. Law § 6527(3).


OVERVIEW: The patients sought disclosure of information related to hospital committee reports concerning the physician and argued that N.Y. Educ. Law § 6527(3), which exempted such reports from disclosure, violated the patients' due process rights. On review, the court held that the state legislature could reasonably have found that providing confidentiality in peer review sessions would further the legitimate state goal of improving the quality of medical care. The court then held that such a legislative policy outweighed the patients' need for evidence to prove their cause of action. Thus, the court held that the statute was constitutionally valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order referring information patients sought in discovery for review to determine if the information was exempted from disclosure.

Associated Builders & Contractors, Inc., Empire State Chapter v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


112 A.D.2d 697; 491 N.Y.S.2d 871; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 56208



July 12, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Upon reargument, judgment modified, on the law, to declare Ordinance 82-450 constitutional and, as modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On reargument of appeal from the judgment of Supreme Court, Monroe County, (New York), appellant contractor sought to declare Rochester, N.Y. Ordinance 82-450 unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the contractor could not, by substituting cash payments or contributions of equivalent value, satisfy the ordinance requirement that it maintain registered apprenticeship training programs. The contractor did not maintain registered apprenticeship training programs. Nor could it be said that allowing cash payments in lieu of maintaining the training programs was consistent with the enunciated policies underlying the legislation pertaining to the apprentice training programs. N.Y. Lab. Law § 810. The contractor could not complain that by operation of the ordinance it was being arbitrarily eliminated from the class of prospective bidders or that it was the object of unfair discriminatory treatment in the public bidding process in contravention of N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 103. The court held that Rochester, N.Y. Ordinance 82-450 was constitutional and did not conflict with N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 103.


OUTCOME: Upon reargument, the judgment was modified, on the law, to declare the ordinance constitutional and, as modified, affirmed.

 

683 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gill


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


109 A.D.2d 419; 491 N.Y.S.2d 524; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 48207



July 11, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, on the law, by vacating the sentence; matter remitted to the County Court of Albany County for resentencing in accordance herewith; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Albany County Court (New York) that convicted defendant, upon his plea of guilty, of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree and sentenced him as a habitual offender.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to possession of a weapon, a felony in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 265.02(1) due to defendant's two prior California convictions. The special information required by N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 200.60 (1) and (2) was not filed when defendant entered his plea, but the lower court adjudged defendant a persistent felony offender under N.Y. Penal Law § 70.10. Defendant appealed, claiming the failure of the prosecution to file the special information resulted in a misdemeanor conviction. The court affirmed, finding the failure a procedural defect that was waived by defendant's voluntary plea. The court found that the two California convictions could not be considered predicate felonies under New York law, however. Therefore, the court ruled that defendant was improperly adjudicated a persistent felony offender, vacated his sentence, and remanded the matter for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court modified defendant's conviction by vacating his adjudication as a persistent felony offender since the two prior California convictions did not constitute predicate felony offenses that could be used by New York and his sentence was vacated with the matter remanded for resentencing.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Proof of Prior Convictions -- Failure to File Special Information

1. The failure of the prosecution to file the special information required by CPL 200.60 (1, 2) alleging defendant's conviction of a crime prior to his plea of guilty to criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree as a felony, due to his previous conviction of a crime, did not result in his conviction of a misdemeanor only; the failure was merely a procedural defect or irregularity which was waived by defendant's voluntary plea. The designation of the charge of criminal possession in the indictment as a felony fully informed defendant of the grade of crime to which he was pleading.

Crimes -- Sentence -- Persistent Felony Offender

2. Defendant's prior California convictions of burglary and grand [***2] theft were improperly considered previous felony convictions forming the predicate for his persistent felony offender status within the meaning of Penal Law § 70.10; in order to avoid the consequences of the unconstitutionality of the statute, all of the statutory elements of the out-of-State convictions must, if admitted by a plea, constitute a felony in New York. Inasmuch as a plea to a felony under the California burglary statute would not in all cases be a plea to a felony in New York, since the statutes do not match, the California conviction cannot be so considered; similarly, the California grand theft statute under which defendant was convicted provided that the value of the property stolen must exceed $ 200 and under New York law, grand larceny, as a class E felony, occurs when the value of the stolen property exceeds $ 250; because a court cannot go behind the statute to which defendant pleaded this California conviction also cannot be considered a predicate felony.

Crimes -- Right to Counsel -- Effective Representation

3. A judgment convicting defendant, upon his plea of guilty, of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree, is modified by vacating [***3] his adjudication as a persistent felony offender and otherwise affirmed; although consideration of defendant's prior California convictions would be an unconstitutional application of Penal Law § 70.10, defendant's failure to succeed in this regard at the hearing in County Court did not result in a denial of the effective assistance of counsel; a review of the plea, the evidence presented at the persistent felony offender hearing, the conduct of counsel and the law compels the conclusion that defendant was adequately provided with meaningful representation.

 

684 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Suffolk Housing Services v. Brookhaven


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


109 A.D.2d 323; 491 N.Y.S.2d 396; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 49754



July 8, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, dated October 20, 1982, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, organizations concerned with low-to-moderate-income housing and racial integration, low-to-moderate-income and minority residents of a town, and taxpayers, sought review of an order from the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which awarded judgment to defendant town in an action challenging the constitutionality of a zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, organizations concerned with low-to-moderate-income housing and racial integration, low-to-moderate-income and minority residents of the town, and taxpayers, filed an action against the town and its officials, alleging that the town, through its zoning ordinances, policies, and practices, had prevented the development of sufficient housing to accommodate its steadily increasing low-to-moderate-income population. The trial court rejected plaintiffs' contentions in their entirety. On appeal, the court found that plaintiffs were unable to demonstrate that the ordinance in question failed to provide for a well-ordered plan for the community. The court further found that plaintiffs failed to prove that the ordinance was unconstitutional because it failed to facilitate the development of low-to-moderate-income housing. Finally, the court found that plaintiffs failed to prove a statutory violation of the Fair Housing Act of 1968, 42 U.S.C.S. § 3601 et seq., in the town's processing of applications to develop such housing.


OUTCOME: The order awarding judgment to the town was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

1. The doctrine articulated by the Court of Appeals in Berenson v Town of New Castle (38 NY2d 102) does not impose a constitutional duty upon a municipality to exercise its zoning powers in order to facilitate the development of low-to-moderate-income or low-cost housing; that doctrine merely requires that a town allow for the construction of different types of housing in sufficient numbers for those people who want and can afford it. Accordingly, the Town of Brookhaven's zoning ordinance meets the Berenson test, since it allows for a wide variety of different types and densities of residential housing and provides a "balanced and well ordered plan for the community"; further, taking into account the number of housing units which the zoning ordinance makes possible and [***2] the number of people who could conceivably be housed therein, the ordinance, on its face, has given more than adequate consideration to local and regional housing needs. Although all housing other than single-family homes requires a special permit, the numbers of permit approvals for such construction are sufficient to show that the special permit procedure has not been employed as a ruse to prevent the construction of multifamily housing in the Town of Brookhaven.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

2. The actions taken by defendant Town of Brookhaven with regard to various applications to develop Federally subsidized multiple-family housing for low-to-moderate-income families did not constitute a violation of the Federal Fair Housing Act (42 USC § 3601 et seq.) which would entitle plaintiffs to the relief they seek, i.e., in addition to rezoning and other related relief, that the town be ordered to commence an affirmative action program to assist developers in obtaining government subsidies to insure the construction of integrated housing. Although 61.2% of the town's black school population was, at the time in issue, located in two communities which contained only [***3] 11% of the town's white population, there were also significant numbers of blacks in other areas of the town; further, while town officials, with popular support, made every effort to exclude low-to-moderate-income housing, there is no direct evidence of any racial motivation on their part.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

3. The special permit application procedures for the establishment of multifamily housing and mobile home communities within defendant Town of Brookhaven are not unconstitutional; although the rigidity of the application process, together with the low and steadily declining rate of approvals is, at least, a contributing cause of the declining rate of applications under the "MF" provisions of the Brookhaven zoning ordinance, plaintiffs have not succeeded in overcoming the presumption of this procedure's constitutionality beyond a reasonable doubt, especially since the town has proffered reasons in support thereof which, if not compelling, are, at least rational. Moreover, on the issue of causation, plaintiffs have failed to refute defendants' contention that economic conditions beyond the town's control are at least equally responsible for the present [***4] situation and that, other factors remaining equal, premapping of land within the town for multifamily usage would not significantly increase the supply of such housing.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

4. The multifamily provisions of the zoning ordinance of defendant Town of Brookhaven, which require additional area for units with extra bedrooms, are not improper; defendants proffered a rational explanation for this rule, since more bedrooms mean more residents and increased population requires increased area. In addition, defendants have demonstrated a rational basis for their 50-acre minimum site area and large recreational area requirements in mobile home communities, and have overcome plaintiffs' further attack upon the town's failure to zone any significant amount of vacant land for any single-family residences on smaller than one third of an acre lots by pointing to the large amount of vacant residential acreage. Finally, plaintiffs' challenge to the alleged practice of imposing covenants, e.g., limiting the number of two-bedroom units in multifamily developments to 20% of the total and requiring that all of the units in "cluster" developments be sold as condominiums [***5] rather than rented, must be rejected in the context of the present challenge to the facial validity of the Brookhaven ordinance under the standard articulated in Berenson v Town of New Castle (38 NY2d 102).

 

685 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Poggi v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


109 A.D.2d 265; 491 N.Y.S.2d 331; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 49750



July 2, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on August 11, 1982, modified, on the law, without costs and without disbursements, the cross motions granted, Administrative Code § B18-27.1 (d) (1) declared constitutional, and, except as thus modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff retired superior police officers sought to enjoin enforcement of a provision of New York City's administrative code, which concerned the police department's pension fund. Defendants were the city and the pension fund's officers. The Patrolmen's Benevolent Association (PBA) intervened in the action. The parties all appealed from an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which denied their motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The code provision established a formula by which certain investment earnings of the pension fund were to be transferred to and allocated between a fund for superior officers and a fund for patrolmen. The PBA had intervened in support of the position of the city and the pension fund officials in the suit, which challenged the provision as violative of the state constitution, as well as an unconstitutional denial of equal protection. The court rejected the claims, and so reversed the denial of summary judgment for the city and the pension fund officials and for the PBA. The court held that the provision did not impair the benefits of the pension contract relationship in that the retired officers could not show that they received less than the full amounts of their retirement allowances. Therefore, the provision did not violate the state constitution. As to the equal protection claim, the court held that the code section, subject only to a rational basis analysis, accomplished the legitimate objective of providing larger supplements to the group with the greater need--the patrolmen-- and, therefore, did not deny the superior officers equal protection of the laws.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's decision and granted summary judgment for the city and the pension fund officials and for the PBA.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Pension and Retirement Benefits -- Variable Supplements Funds

1. Administrative Code of the City of New York § B18-27.1 (d) (1), which establishes a formula by which certain investment earnings of the Police Pension Fund are to be transferred to and allocated between the Police Superior Officers' Variable Supplements Fund and the Patrolmen's Variable Supplements Fund, in no way diminishes or impairs any benefit set forth in the statutory Pension Fund contract of the Administrative Code and, therefore, does not violate NY Constitution, article V, § 7; the variable supplement [***2] payments are independent of, and were not intended to diminish or impair, any benefit under the Pension Fund contract; further, even assuming that superior officers do contribute more over a career to the Pension Fund than patrolmen, superior officers are not entitled to a greater share of the transferable earnings than that provided in section B18-27.1 (d) (1) since the statutory pension contract does not contain any benefit provision which requires future supplement payments to be calculated on the basis of length of service. Moreover, except to the extent that it is required to pay interest on members' accumulated contributions (Administrative Code § B18-29.0), the Pension Fund is not required by any provision of the statutory pension contract to pay investment earnings to its members.

Municipal Corporations -- Pension and Retirement Benefits -- Variable Supplements Funds -- Equal Protection of Laws

2. Administrative Code of the City of New York § B18-27.1 (d) (1) does not deny equal protection of the laws to superior officers of the New York City Police Department by providing a formula for the allocation of transferable earnings between the Police Superior Officers'  [***3] Variable Supplements Fund (SOVSF) and the Patrolmen's Variable Supplements Fund (PVSF) which results in the payment by the PVSF of larger nonpension supplemental benefits to eligible retired patrolmen than the SOVSF is able to pay to eligible retired superior officers. The allocation formula of section B18-27.1 (d) (1) classifies benefits not merely on the basis of rank, but, also, on need; the Legislature could rationally and reasonably find that retired patrolmen, who, on the average, receive smaller service retirement allowances than retired superior officers, have greater need for the larger supplemental payments than retired superior officers; a statute which allocates larger monetary benefits to those with greater need does not violate the equal protection clause because such a classification is rationally related to a legitimate State interest.

Rodgers v. Koch


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


111 A.D.2d 727; 491 N.Y.S.2d 181; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 49970



June 27, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, public library archivist, public library, public library support foundations, the University of the State of New York, and the Education Department of the State of New York, challenged an order from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which denied appellants' motions to dismiss the action brought by respondent citizens seeking declaratory relief that the archivist's appointment was discriminatory and improper.


OVERVIEW: Appellant archivist, a Caucasian male who held a doctorate in history and was versed in French and Spanish and had a background in Latin, was appointed to the position of archivist for the public library. The position was not offered to the assistant archivist, a black female, because she was considered unqualified as she could not speak French. The lower court had granted the motions to dismiss filed by several defendants, but not appellants. On appeal, the court concluded that appellants were entitled to dismissal of the citizens' action. The court held that the citizens lacked standing to interpose themselves and the courts into the management and operation of the public library as there was no claim of unconstitutionality or other clear illegality and as the citizens were not within a zone of interest protected by legislation. The court found that the Commissioner of Education of the State of New York had the authority to oversee the functioning of the library system and to determine whether a staff member of the library must have met specific qualification or if those requirements were waivable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment denying appellants' motions to dismiss and dismissed the citizens' action.

Umansky v. New York Metropolitan Transit Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


111 A.D.2d 918; 491 N.Y.S.2d 33; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 50185
