People v. Fiorvante


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


108 A.D.2d 925; 485 N.Y.S.2d 816; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 43260



February 25, 1985 

 

717 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Berwick v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


107 A.D.2d 79; 486 N.Y.S.2d 260; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 48418



February 25, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Three judgments of the Court of Claims dated May 19, 1982 (claim No. 3), May 19, 1982 (claim No. 4) and June 14, 1982 (claim No. 5), respectively, affirmed, without costs or disbursements.

Three judgments of the Court of Claims dated May 20, 1982 (claim No. 1), June 14, 1982 (claim No. 2), and May 19, 1982 (claim No. 6), respectively, reversed, on the law and the facts, without costs or disbursements, and matters remitted to the Court of Claims for a new hearing limited solely to the amount to be awarded the claimants based on an increment above the recreational value that a knowledgeable buyer would pay in light of the reasonable probability of a successful constitutional challenge in court to applicable legislation restricting the use of tidal wetlands.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimants sought review of the order of the Court of Claims (New York), which held that the highest and best use of claimants' properties was recreational, agreeing with the opinion of respondent state's appraiser that residential development was not economically feasible.


OVERVIEW: The central issue on appeal was the proper valuation of property classified as wetlands taken in condemnation where the claim was made that the wetlands restrictions were, themselves, confiscatory. The claimants argued that the court of claims' finding as to the effect of the Tidal Wetlands Act was erroneous in that the properties had to be valued without regard to the Act if they had a reasonable economic use; and that the court erred in finding that zoning changes were required before the properties could have been residentially developed. The court did not agree with claimants that differences in size, access and zoning among the subject parcels necessitated a different per acre market value for each. The court accepted the court of claims' finding of a $ 3,000 per acre market value for some parcels as accurately representing their market value, at the time of taking, as a result of regulation by the Tidal Wetlands Act. For the remainder of the parcels, the court found that the claimants did establish that development was economically feasible and that a successful court challenge to application of the Tidal Wetlands Act as confiscatory was reasonably probable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, and reversed in part, the judgment of the court of claims and remitted some of the claims for a new hearing limited solely to the amount to be awarded the claimants based on an increment above the recreational value that a knowledgeable buyer would pay in light of the reasonable probability of a successful constitutional challenge in court to applicable legislation restricting the use of tidal wetlands.


HEADNOTES

Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Tidal Wetlands

1. Property taken in condemnation must be valued as legally restricted in use by all zoning and environmental [***2] regulations in effect on the date of taking; if a claimant believes that application of the Tidal Wetlands Act ( ECL, art 25) unconstitutionally deprives him of any reasonable economic return on his property, he may challenge its validity in the condemnation proceeding by demonstrating that no permit would have issued for any economically productive use and that application of the Act, coupled with the denial of a permit, destroyed the property's economic value; however, the law accords no retroactive effect to an evidentiary showing that claimant would probably have succeeded in securing a judicial declaration of unconstitutionality had he challenged the denial of a permit. Accordingly, in an action involving the valuation of property classified as wetlands taken in condemnation, where the claim is made that the wetlands restrictions are, themselves, confiscatory, the Court of Claims did not err in its statement that difficulties in securing approval for residential development under local and State tidal wetlands regulations would reduce the desirability of the properties in the marketplace; that claimants might eventually have secured a judicial declaration of confiscation, compelling [***3] the State to compensate them for their loss if a permit was denied, cannot be translated into a market value for immediate residential use on the date of taking.

Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Tidal Wetlands -- Feasibility of Residential Development

2. In an action involving the proper valuation of property classified as wetlands taken in condemnation, where it is claimed that the wetlands restrictions are, themselves, confiscatory, it was error to find that residential development of two properties was not economically feasible. Even assuming the necessity of a sewage treatment plant for the proposed condominium units on the properties, the State's appraiser's conclusion that development costs would thereby become prohibitive is unsupported; there is a strong demand for residential property in the village where the properties are located, high prices are being paid therefor and the properties are subject to premium high density zoning; thus, but for the strictures of the Tidal Wetlands Act ( ECL, art 25), development would have been economically feasible, and the highest and best use of the properties was therefore residential, not recreational.

Eminent Domain  [***4] -- Compensation -- Tidal Wetlands -- Feasibility of Residential Development

3. In an action involving the proper valuation of property classified as wetlands taken in condemnation, where it is claimed that the wetlands restrictions are, themselves, confiscatory, the Court of Claims went contrary to the weight of the evidence when it found residential development of one parcel of property to be economically unfeasible; the development costs proffered by claimants are supported by the testimony of an engineer with prior experience in developing property of a similar nature; the huge development costs proffered by the State are totally arbitrary. The State had neither an engineering survey of the property, test borings, nor land use regulations to support its thesis; the State's failure to buttress its position with engineering testimony renders its conclusion entirely unconvincing.

Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Tidal Wetlands -- Feasibility of Residential Development

4. In an action involving the proper valuation of property classified as wetlands taken in condemnation, where it is claimed that the wetlands restrictions are, themselves, confiscatory, claimants have [***5] failed to carry their burden of proof as to the economic feasibility of developing certain parcels of property, each of which is less than 300 feet in width and landlocked but for a one-rod right-of-way, in light of the county's requirement that septic tanks be set back 150 feet from tidal wetlands. Each parcel could never meet that requirement, treated in isolation, because the adjoining parcels would remain tidal wetlands; the costs of the development which would constitute a permissible use under the Tidal Wetlands Act ( ECL, art 25) would be prohibitive if the Act was fully applicable and claimants did not share the cost of improvements common to all; even if all the parcels could be treated as one and/or the setback regulation ignored, and assuming all claimants would have sought to develop their properties at the same time, the claimants' estimated costs and the development cost adjustment to their comparable sales are unrealistically low. Moreover, sales of property which essentially encircles the subject parcels, although showing a relatively large appreciation in land values, do not support claimants' case; said property is not comparable to claimants' parcels since the lots [***6] are not dependent on a right-of-way for access and are located on upland, not wetland.

Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Tidal Wetlands -- Measure of Damages

5. In an action involving the proper valuation of property classified as wetlands taken in condemnation, where it is claimed that the wetlands restrictions are, themselves, confiscatory, the correct measure of the market value at the time of taking is the value of the property as legally restricted in use by the Tidal Wetlands Act ( ECL, art 25) and any other pertinent environmental regulations in effect on the date of taking; accordingly, the Court of Claims properly found a $ 3,000 per acre market value for the subject parcels at the time of taking as a result of regulation by the Tidal Wetlands Act; differences in size, access and zoning among the subject parcels do not necessitate a different per acre market value for each. As to the subject parcels for which residential development was not economically feasible, the wetlands' regulations are not confiscatory and, thus, the court's finding as to market value, as restricted, is conclusive.

Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Tidal Wetlands -- Incremental Award

 [***7] 6. In an action involving the proper valuation of property classified as wetlands taken in condemnation, where it is claimed that the wetlands restrictions are, themselves, confiscatory, in regard to the subject parcels as to which a comparison of market values with and without regard to the developmental restrictions of the Tidal Wetlands Act ( ECL, art 25) demonstrates that application of the Act has effectively deprived those claimants of all financially rewarding uses of their property, a constitutional challenge to application of the Act as confiscatory would have a reasonable probability of success; accordingly, those claimants are entitled to an incremental award, over and above the property's restricted market value, representing its enhanced value to a knowledgeable buyer in light of the reasonable probability of a successful constitutional challenge.

Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Tidal Wetlands -- Market Value -- Prior Arm's Length Sale of Parcel

7. In an action involving the proper valuation of property classified as wetlands taken in condemnation, where it is claimed that the wetlands restrictions are, themselves, confiscatory, it was not error to totally [***8] disregard a prior arm's length sale of one of the subject parcels as the best comparable for purposes of determining market value on the day of taking. Although generally, the price of a prior arm's length sale of property is, prima facie, an excellent indicator of its value at that time, the prior sale of the subject parcel in 1968 is of little value in determining the market value of the property in 1979, the time of taking; it has no bearing on the market value of the property as restricted by the Tidal Wetlands Act ( ECL, art 25) since the Act did not exist at the time of the sale; nor would it appear to have much relevance in connection with the incremental award to be made upon remand.

Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Tidal Wetlands -- Feasibility of Development

8. In an action involving the proper valuation of property classified as wetlands taken in condemnation, where it is claimed that the wetlands restrictions are, themselves, confiscatory, topographic maps of property which was very similar to one of the subject parcels should have been allowed into evidence on the issue of the economic feasibility of development; since the material was not offered as direct [***9] evidence of market value via comparable sales, distant location would not be a disability; to the extent that said maps could aid in determining what would be necessary physically to develop the subject parcel and what the cost might be, they would be relevant and helpful.

 

718 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Consolidated Edison Co. v. Public Service Com.


No. 48568


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


107 A.D.2d 73; 485 N.Y.S.2d 607; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 42526



February 21, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, electric and gas utilities, sought review of a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which denied petitioners' complaint to annul an order by respondent, the Public Service Commission of the State of New York (PSC), that determined that petitioners were required to pay 50 percent of the costs of mailing if the mail inserts contained messages that expressed the views or opinions of petitioners.


OVERVIEW: A proceeding was commenced by the PSC that culminated in an order that determined that petitioners were required to pay 50 percent of the costs of mailing if the mail inserts contained views or opinions of petitioners. Petitioners brought an action that sought an annulment of the PSC order and an action for declaratory judgment to declare that the order violated the United States Constitution. The trial court denied the relief sought by petitioners. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision and held that the order was not a content-based restriction and thus did not need to provide a precisely drawn method of serving a compelling state interest. The court ruled that although petitioners were clearly entitled to insert printed materials in the billing envelopes that expressed their views, no corresponding duty existed on the part of the government or the ratepayers to bear the cost burdens necessary to realize that right. The court also held that the cost allocation formula adopted by the PSC was not an unreasonable means of treating costs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of petitioners' action to annul the order of the PSC that determined petitioners were required to pay 50 percent of the costs of mailing if the mail inserts contained messages that expressed the views or opinions of petitioners.


HEADNOTES

Public Utilities -- Cost of Customer Service Bill Inserts -- Expression of Utility's Views on Controversial Issues

The Public Service Commission of the State of New York (PSC) cannot prohibit utility companies from including inserts which express the views or opinions of the utility on controversial issues of public policy in envelopes containing service bills to customers (U.S. Const, 1st amend). However, the utility companies' right to freedom of speech is not abridged by a PSC order directing that shareholders and ratepayers share on a 50-50 basis the costs of such mailings, including stamps, envelopes and processing costs and the costs of assembling and updating ratepayers mailing lists; the order is not a content-based restriction [***2] and, thus, need not provide a precisely drawn method of serving a compelling State interest. Further, the PSC is empowered to consider the "nonutility" use of assets, including a utility plant or the nature of company expenditures, and to take appropriate rate-making action to ensure that ratepayers are not compelled to pay for the cost of such activities or expenditures; finally, the cost allocation formula adopted by the PSC is not an unreasonable means of treating costs and should be upheld as proper application of its technical expertise and judgmental powers.

 

719 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Horner v. State


No. 47852


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


107 A.D.2d 64; 485 N.Y.S.2d 595; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 42528



February 21, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged an order from the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which granted a motion by respondent taxpayer for a preliminary injunction and denied a cross motion by the state for an order dismissing the complaint. The taxpayer's request for an injunction stemmed from his receipt of two notices of determination and demand for payment of sales and use taxes.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer alleged that he was the owner of 48 of the 200 authorized and issued shares of the corporation's stock, that he was not an officer of the corporation during the period covered by the tax assessment, and that he was never an employee of the corporation. His only contact with the corporation was his act of lending it money. The taxpayer alleged that he was not otherwise involved in either the operation or management of the corporation. The state had sought to hold the taxpayer personally liable for the taxes owed by the corporation pursuant to N.Y. Tax. Law §§ 1131(1), 1133, in accordance with N.Y. Tax Law § 1138(a). The taxpayer declined administrative review and filed his declaratory judgment action. The lower court granted the taxpayer a preliminary injunction to restrain the state from assessing of attempting to collect the taxes from him personally. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that if the taxpayer's allegations were accepted as true, then he was beyond the authority of the state under the tax law. Because the taxing authority's jurisdiction was challenged as inapplicable, review in a manner other than that prescribed in the tax statute was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court, which granted the taxpayer's request for an injunction restraining the state from assessing or attempting to collect the corporation's taxes from the taxpayer personally.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Judicial Review -- Action beyond Authority of Taxing Authority

Administrative procedures are not the exclusive means for plaintiff to challenge notices of determination and demand for payment of sales and use taxes issued by the Department of Taxation and Finance seeking to hold him personally liable for payment thereof (Tax Law § 1131 [1]; §§ 1133, 1138 [a]), and plaintiff properly sought relief in an action for declaratory judgment where he alleges that while he is the owner of 48 shares of the 200 shares of stock of the subject corporation, he has not been involved in either the operation or management of the corporation in any way; when the taxing authority's jurisdiction is challenged as unconstitutional or inapplicable, as herein, review in a manner other than that prescribed in the tax statute [***2] is appropriate; accordingly, Special Term properly denied defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint. Further, the court was correct in granting plaintiff a preliminary injunction to restrain defendants from taking any action under the provisions of the Tax Law to collect the taxes they claim are due.

People v. Thomas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


108 A.D.2d 884; 485 N.Y.S.2d 579; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 43215



February 19, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York), which, upon a jury verdict, convicted him of grand larceny in the first degree (two counts), and sentenced him, as a second felony offender, to concurrent indeterminate terms of three to six years' imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was incarcerated when it was alleged that he had extorted money from fellow inmates and was indicted. Defendant was sentenced as a second felony offender to the minimum sentence authorized by N.Y. Penal Law § 70.06. The court affirmed his conviction and sentence because the evidence was sufficient to establish that defendant had extorted money from fellow inmates. The court stated that defendant's claim, raised for the first time on appeal, that the sentencing statute was unconstitutional as applied to him and that under the circumstances the mandatory minimum authorized sentence of three to six years was cruel and unusual punishment, was not preserved for review. The court found that there were no exceptional circumstances to support such a claim. The court noted that because defendant's moving papers did not make a prima facie showing of some compelling factor, consideration, or circumstance clearly demonstrating that his prosecution and conviction were unjust, there was no legal basis for dismissal of the indictment in the furtherance of justice, and it was not error for the court to deny defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment without having conducted a hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

People v. De Santis

723 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Shad Alliance v. Smith Haven Mall


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


106 A.D.2d 189; 484 N.Y.S.2d 849; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 52058



February 4, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment (one paper) of the Supreme Court, Suffolk County, dated April 4, 1983, affirmed, with costs payable by the appellant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant mall challenged the decision of the Supreme Court, Special Term, Suffolk County (New York), which (1) denied its motion for summary judgment, and (2) granted a cross motion by plaintiff organization for summary judgment. The trial court held that association's members had a right under N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 8, 9 to communicate their ideas in the mall.


OVERVIEW: The issue to be decided was whether the rights of free speech and petition, N.Y. Const. art I, §§ 8 and 9, protected association's members who wished to distribute leaflets dealing with matters of public interest and concern at a large, privately owned suburban shopping mall. The court concluded that the mall, by virtue of its size, location, and mode of operation, had undertaken the character and attributes of a downtown business district or town center. Thus, the rights of free expression under §§ 8 and 9 required that the mall be enjoined from prohibiting the distribution of leaflets on its premises, subject only to the adoption of reasonable regulations as to the time, place, and manner in which such activities may be carried out so as to minimize any interference with the mall's commercial functions. The mall was a property which had to make an accommodation to the exercise of free speech by the general public, in this case expression with respect to the sharply debated but vitally important issues of nuclear power and nuclear weaponry. Full expression of ideas in those areas was not only desirable but was to be encouraged.


OUTCOME: The order and judgment was affirmed, with costs payable by the mall. The policy of mall's management prohibiting leafleting denied the citizens of New York the right of free expression enjoyed by them under the New York Constitution.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Freedom of Assembly -- Leafleting at Privately Owned Shopping Mall

Defendant, a large, privately owned and operated suburban shopping mall, has undertaken the character and attributes of a downtown business district or town center by virtue of its size, location and mode of operation, such that the rights of free expression under the NY Constitution,  [***2] article I, §§ 8, 9 require that the mall be enjoined from prohibiting the distribution of leaflets on its premises, subject only to the adoption of reasonable regulations as to the time, place and manner in which such activities may be carried out so as to minimize any interference with the mall's commercial functions. Although leafleting at a privately owned shopping center, regardless of its size or character, is not a protected activity under the US Constitution, 1st Amendment, there is no Federal constitutional bar to a State according its citizens the right to engage in expressive activity in such a shopping center; such activity breaches neither the property rights nor the 1st Amendment rights of the owner of the shopping center.

People v. Singleton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


107 A.D.2d 828; 484 N.Y.S.2d 668; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 42754



January 28, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of three judgments by the Supreme Court, Queens County, New York, which, upon his guilty plea, convicted him of third and second degree robbery and first degree attempted robbery. Defendant alleged that his guilty pleas should be vacated because he was not advised of his rights to have witnesses testify in his defense and to have his guilt proven beyond a reasonable doubt to a unanimous jury.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that when he submitted his guilty pleas he was not told of his right to have witnesses testify in his defense and to make the state prove his guilt beyond a reasonable doubt to a unanimous jury. Defendant also alleged that his sentencing as a second felony offender statute, as agreed on as part of the plea agreement, was inappropriate and that the second felony offender statute was unconstitutional. Finally, defendant claimed that his sentence was unduly harsh and excessive. The court affirmed the trial court's convictions and sentences of defendant. The court held that because defendant failed to withdraw his pleas on the grounds that he was not properly advised by the trial court of his rights during his plea allocution, consistent with N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 440.10, he waived those claims for appellate review. The court also held that his sentence was appropriate and not unduly harsh. The court also ruled that defendant's failure to raise his objection to his sentence as a second felony offender to the trial court waived that claim and that the second felony offender statute had been ruled as constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgments convicting defendant of robbery in the third degree, robbery in the second degree, and attempted robbery in the first degree and imposing sentences for those crimes.

726 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Am. Ass'n of Bioanalysts v. Axelrod


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


106 A.D.2d 53; 484 N.Y.S.2d 288; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 42546



January 3, 1985 



DISPOSITION: Order in Action No. 1, modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as denied defendant's motion for summary judgment on the first, third and sixth causes of action and denied plaintiff's cross motion for summary judgment on its fourth cause of action; summary judgment granted to the respective parties on said causes of action, and it is declared that the fee schedule was not properly promulgated and filed and, accordingly, is ineffective; and, as so modified, affirmed.

Order in Action No. 2, modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as denied defendants'  [***2] cross motion for summary judgment on plaintiffs' sixth and ninth causes of action; summary judgment granted to defendants on said causes of action, summary judgment granted to plaintiffs on their second cause of action, and it is declared that the fee schedule was not properly promulgated and filed and, accordingly, is ineffective; and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff laboratories and defendant commissioner of the department of health (department) sought review of judgments from the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which in the first action, granted the laboratory's motion for discovery and denied both parties' cross motions for summary judgment and in the second, granted the laboratory's motion for discovery and denied the department's cross motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The laboratories were billed for laboratory inspection and reference fees by the department on a sliding-scale fee schedule. The laboratories filed actions for declaratory and injunctive relief, claiming that N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 576(4) was unconstitutional, and seeking to enjoin the department from enforcing the fee schedule. The trial court granted the first laboratory's injunction, granted both laboratories' motions for discovery, and denied all parties' motions for summary judgment. The actions were consolidated, all parties appealed, and the court modified both judgments, granting in part the department's motions for summary judgment. The court held that the legislature had the right to recover the cost of a regulatory program prior to those costs being incurred as long as the fee schedule was based upon ever-changing estimated costs, and as such, § 576(4) was constitutional. The court concluded, however, that because the fee schedule was not properly promulgated and filed, it was ineffective regardless of any knowledge the laboratories had of it because the department could not retroactively benefit from an amended regulation not in effect at the time of billing.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's orders in both actions, granting in part the department's motions for summary judgment, but technically determining the appeal in the laboratories' favor in their actions against the department seeking declaratory and injunctive relief from the department's enforcement of laboratory inspection and reference fees based on a sliding-scale fee schedule.


HEADNOTES

Health -- Clinical Laboratories -- Inspection and Reference Fees Imposed by State

1. A statute (Public Health Law, § 576, subd 4) which permits the establishment of a sliding scale fee schedule whereby the Department of Health may bill clinical laboratories for inspection and reference fees does not impose a tax in spite of the absence of a provision for the return of amounts collected in excess of the State's actual cost, where the only support for the contention that the amount of fees collected exceeded the actual cost was the fact that the Director of the Budget increased the Health Department's estimated cost from $ 1.8 million to $ 2.5 million, since that does not establish that the fee schedule imposes a tax; the purpose of the statute is to recover the cost of regulating clinical laboratories, not to raise [***3] revenue for the support of government generally.

Health -- Clinical Laboratories -- Inspection and Reference Fees Imposed by State

2. A statute (Public Health Law, § 576, subd 4) which requires the Department of Health to adopt rules and regulations establishing schedules for inspection and laboratory reference fees in amounts not exceeding the estimated cost of the department's program, does not unconstitutionally fail to articulate objective standards delimiting the department's discretion in establishing the fee schedule since the statute's purpose to have the industry bear the cost of the subject program and the implied reasonableness standard provide sufficient standards. There is no unconstitutional delegation of lawmaking authority where the statute is unambiguous as to what power is being conferred, there being no prior action by the Legislature indicating an intent to retain authority to fix fees, where the fee schedule must be established so as to recover no more than the reasonable estimated costs of operating the program, and where the fee charged to each individual laboratory must be reasonable and equitably allocated.

Health -- Clinical Laboratories  [***4] -- Inspection and Reference Fees Imposed by State -- Equal Protection

3. A sliding scale fee schedule established by the Department of Health to recover its costs in administering a laboratory inspection and reference program, whereby each laboratory's fee is based upon the annual volume of tests it performs, does not offend equal protection; equal protection does not require that all classifications be made with mathematical precision.

Health -- Clinical Laboratories -- Inspection and Reference Fees Imposed by State

4. A schedule of fees, which may be charged by the Department of Health to recover the costs of a laboratory inspection and reference program, was required by the enabling statute (Public Health Law, § 576, subd 4) to be filed and promulgated together with the regulations of the Department of Health which implemented the statute, and since the fee schedule was not properly promulgated and filed, it is ineffective regardless of any knowledge plaintiff laboratories, which are challenging the validity of the schedule, may have had of it.

 

727 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Talbert


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


107 A.D.2d 842; 484 N.Y.S.2d 680; 1985 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 42766



January 3, 1985 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the County Court of Albany County (New York), which convicted defendant upon his plea of guilty of the crime of attempted criminal possession of a controlled substance in the fourth degree, contending that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress.


OVERVIEW: The object upon which the officer based the arrest and subsequent search of defendant in which a controlled substance was found was described in the record as a broom handle with tape wrapped around each end. Possession of a billy was prohibited by N.Y. Penal Law § 265.01(1). The fact that the term "billy" had to be strictly construed to mean a heavy wooden stick with a handle grip that was designed to be used to strike an individual did not mean that only a commercially manufactured nightstick fit the definition. A homemade object, even one fashioned from as innocent an item as a broom could be a billy. The record description indicated that the tape around each end provided a handle grip. An object that could be used as a billy but which did not fit the strict definition could still be a prohibited weapon under § 265.01(2).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

728 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


International Paper Co. v. Sterling Forest Pollution Control Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


105 A.D.2d 278; 482 N.Y.S.2d 827; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20698



December 31, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment (one paper) of the Supreme Court, Orange County, dated August 22, 1983, affirmed, with one bill of costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff waste producer challenged an order and judgment from the Supreme Court at Special Term, Orange County (New York), which dismissed its complaint as barred by the statute of limitations found in N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217. The waste producer sought a judgment declaring that a stipulation between defendants sewerage-works corporation and town violated N.Y. Transp. Corp. Law § 121 and declaring the town's resolution approving the resolution invalid.


OVERVIEW: The town and a sewage-works corporation entered into a stipulation of settlement that set the rates to be charged to a waste producer for a three-year period. The waste producer commenced an action seeking a judgment declaring the stipulation of settlement and the town's resolution approving the settlement violative of § 121 and seeking a judgment declaring that fair, reasonable, and adequate rates be set. The trial court dismissed the complaint on the ground that it was barred by the four-month statute of limitations. On appeal, the court affirmed the dismissal. The court held that the challenged action was administrative in nature, not legislative. Therefore, the challenged action was reviewable in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding, for which a four-month time limitation was prescribed by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217. The factors that led the court to conclude that the challenged action was administrative rather than legislative were that the action was addressed to individualized rates established for the waste producer, the stipulation covered a specified time period, and the action challenged was taken informally by means of stipulation and resolution, not by legislative enactment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order and judgment granting the motion to dismiss the waste producer's complaint on the ground that the action was barred by the statute of limitations.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions -- What Statute Governs -- Review of Sewerage Rates

Plaintiff's failure to commence within the time prescribed in CPLR 217 for the review of an administrative action, i.e., four months, a declaratory judgment action to review a stipulation between defendants setting sewerage rates to be charged plaintiff pursuant to section 121 of the Transportation Corporations Law, requires dismissal of the action since the defendants' action, finalized by a resolution of defendant Town Board, was administrative, rather than legislative, in nature. The challenge is addressed to individualized rates established for a particular litigant; defendants' action was for a limited duration covering a specified time period; and the action challenged was taken informally by means of stipulation and resolution by the local [***2] governing body, rather than by formal enactment of legislation. The fact that the action was taken pursuant to a statute, which did not require notice or a hearing, is of no legal significance in light of those factors found to be characteristic of administrative action, viz., individualized application, limited duration and informal adoption.

 

729 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Equine Practitioners Ass'n v. New York State Racing & Wagering Bd.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


105 A.D.2d 215; 483 N.Y.S.2d 239; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20691



December 20, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order, Supreme Court, New York County, entered on October 26, 1983, unanimously modified, on the law, to grant defendant's motion and declare that the challenged regulations herein were within the defendant Board's statutory authority to promulgate, and were rationally based and constitutional, and otherwise affirmed, without costs and without disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, New York State Racing and Wagering Board (board), challenged an order from the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which denied a motion by the board to dismiss the complaint of plaintiff veterinarians, alleging that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, §§ 4012, 4043, 4120, which restricted the administration of drugs to racehorses, were unconstitutional and a usurpation of the board's power.


OVERVIEW: The veterinarians sought declaratory and injunctive relief against the board after the board promulgated new regulations restricting the administration of drugs to racehorses prior to competition, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, §§ 4012, 4043, 4120. The veterinarians contended that the regulations arbitrarily distinguished among various drugs as to time and method of administration and, thus, constituted a denial of due process of law. They also asserted that the new rules improperly attempted to regulate the practice of veterinary medicine, thereby exceeding the board's statutory authority. Upon a motion to dismiss filed by the board, the lower court found that issues of fact precluded judgment. The court modified the judgment, however, to enter judgment for the board. The court ruled that there was a rational relationship between the rules and the purpose for which they were adopted. Thus, the court ruled that they did not violate due process. The court also ruled that since the provisions were rationally based, the court could not substitute its judgment for that of the board.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment of the lower court to grant the board's motion to dismiss, finding that the challenged regulations were within the authority of the board to promulgate and were not unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Horse Racing -- Regulations Governing Drug Usage Prior to Race Time -- Statutory Authority

1. A complaint against defendant New York State Racing and Wagering Board challenging the 1982 Board rules governing the administration of drugs prior to race time and alleging that the Board exceeded its statutory authority and usurped powers vested in the Board of Regents by attempting to regulate the practice of veterinary medicine, should be dismissed; pursuant to section 301 [***2] (subd 2, par b) of the Racing, Pari-Mutuel Wagering and Breeding Law, the Board is empowered to prescribe rules and regulations in order to prevent "the use of improper devices, the administration of drugs or stimulants or other improper acts for the purpose of affecting the speed" of the horses about to participate in races. The 1982 rules do not prohibit a licensed veterinarian from administering a drug necessary for the life or health of a horse and the decision to prescribe a drug is left with the veterinarian.

Horse Racing -- Regulations Governing Drug Usage Prior to Race Time -- Veterinarians Not Deprived of Due Process

2. The 1982 regulations of the New York State Racing and Wagering Board, in particular 9 NYCRR 4043.2 and 4120.2, do not arbitrarily distinguish among various drugs as to time schedule and method of administration thus depriving veterinarians of due process of law. The regulations were established based upon the advice of the Board's expert and in response to the demands of the racing industry and under the circumstances, the court may not substitute its judgment for that of the Board where the Board determined that the clearance time of drugs in [***3] a horse's urine is related to the drug's effect upon a horse's performance in a race. There is a rational relationship between the rules and the purpose for which they were adopted and the rules are reasonably related to the promotion of the public welfare.

Horse Racing -- Regulations Providing for Track Inspections of Licensed Persons -- Unreasonable Search and Seizure

3. Sections 4012.1 (d) and 4120.5 (d) of the New York State Racing and Wagering Board's regulations (9 NYCRR 4012.1 [d]; 4120.5 [d]) which provide for track inspections of licensed veterinarians to insure compliance with and deter evasion of the Board's prohibition against possession of hypodermic equipment and controlled substances do not deprive racetrack veterinarians of their constitutional right to be free from unreasonable search and seizure and do not amount to an impermissible delegation of authority by the Board. The Board, having promulgated regulations, is empowered to attach certain conditions to licenses issued to equine veterinarians in order effectively to implement those regulations. The inspections are an essential part of the regulatory scheme.

Horse Racing -- Regulations  [***4] Allowing Suspension of Trainer without Hearing -- Due Process

4. The 1982 regulations of the New York State Racing and Wagering Board (9 NYCRR parts 4120, 4013, 4043) concerning the responsibilities imposed upon trainers and which authorize the Board to suspend a trainer without a hearing do not deprive trainers of due process; subdivision 3 of section 401 of the State Administrative Procedure Act expressly permits a prehearing suspension in an emergency, which action may be challenged in a CPLR article 78 proceeding, and the United States Supreme Court has held that a summary suspension based upon a rebuttable presumption of trainer responsibility when the State has probable cause to believe that a horse has been drugged and that the trainer has been, at least, negligent does not violate due process.

 

730 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Miller


[N0 NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


106 A.D.2d 787; 484 N.Y.S.2d 183; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21710



December 20, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a prison inmate, appealed a judgment of the County Court of Chemung County (New York.), which was rendered upon a verdict convicting defendant of violating N.Y. Penal Law § 205.25, which was the crime of promoting prison contraband in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violating § 205.25 as the result of his possession of a metal rod with a sharpened end called a "shank." Defendant argued that the failure to disclose the names and addresses of all potential prosecution witnesses violated his Sixth Amendment right to counsel, that N.Y. Penal Law § 205.25 was unconstitutionally vague, and that the trial court abused its discretion by admitting a chair from the correctional facility into evidence. The court on appeal held that there was neither a constitutional nor statutory obligation mandating the pretrial disclosure of the identity of a prosecution witness. Defendant did not present any special circumstances for the disclosure, and the decision to disallow it was discretionary with the trial court. The court also found that § 205.25 was not constitutionally vague because it was sufficiently explicit to inform a reasonable man of the nature of the offense prohibited. The court held that defendant failed to overcome the presumption of the statute's constitutional validity. The court also held that the chair's admission into evidence was not error because it was probative as to how defendant may have obtained the shank.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which was rendered upon the conviction of defendant for the crime of promoting prison contraband in the first degree.

 

731 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Department of Social Services on behalf of Katherine McL. v. Jay W.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


105 A.D.2d 19; 482 N.Y.S.2d 810; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20673



December 17, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Appeal from an order of the Family Court, Suffolk County, entered January 4, 1982 dismissed, without costs or disbursements. That order is not an order of disposition (Family Ct Act, § 1112); it is brought up for review on the appeal from an order of the same court, entered January 18, 1983.

Order entered January 18, 1983 affirmed, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of an order of the Family Court, Suffolk County (New York), which, in a paternity proceeding, declared appellant to be the father of petitioner's child, and from a subsequent order of that court which directed respondent to pay $ 193.50 per month for the support of the child. The father argued that the first order was entered after the expiration of the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: The mother was designated as an agent of a Michigan "public welfare official" and brought an action against the father to establish paternity more than five years after the birth of the child. The father argued that the paternity proceeding was actually brought by the mother on her own behalf, thus the two-year statute of limitations applied and the order was improperly issued. The court first held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying the father leave to amend his answer to assert the statute of limitations defense. However, even if the issue had been timely raise, the court held that the paternity proceeding was properly viewed as one originated by a public welfare official and was timely under the 10-year statute of limitations applicable thereto as provided under N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 517(b). The mother was a long-term recipient of public assistance and the State of Michigan had an interest in seeking to establish paternity in order to relieve itself of any continuing financial burden. Contrary to the father's contention, there was no attempt to perpetrate a fraud on either him or the court. According, the order establishing paternity was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appeal and affirmed the order of paternity and the order of support.


HEADNOTES

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Statute of Limitations

1. In a paternity proceeding commenced in the name of petitioner mother approximately five and one-half years after the birth of her child out of wedlock the Family Court properly denied a motion by respondent putative father to amend his answer to [***2] assert the two-year Statute of Limitations for paternity proceedings commenced by the mother of a child born out of wedlock which was in effect at the time of the commencement of the proceeding (Family Ct Act, § 517, former subd [a]), since respondent inexcusably failed to make the application until the trial had commenced; accordingly, respondent's failure to raise that defense in a pre-answer motion to dismiss or in his answer constituted a waiver thereof ( CPLR 3211, subd [a], par 5; subd [e]). In any event, since the petition was submitted with a sworn statement by a representative of the Michigan Department of Social Services authorizing petitioner, who was receiving Aid to Dependent Children funds from the State of Michigan, to act as its agent for the purpose of pursuing child support from respondent, the instant paternity proceeding is properly viewed as one originated by a public welfare official and, therefore, it is timely under the 10-year Statute of Limitations applicable to paternity proceedings commenced by such officials (Family Ct Act, § 517, subd [b]); the State of Michigan Department of Social Services is a "public welfare official" for purposes of the [***3] statute (Family Ct Act, §§ 517, 522) and the designation of petitioner mother as the agent of the Michigan Department of Social Services was proper.

Children Born out of Wedlock -- Paternity Proceeding -- Appeal

2. In a paternity proceeding in which the Family Court declared respondent to be the father of petitioner's child, the court should have proceeded to determine as well the issue of support, within the same proceeding (Family Ct Act, § 545); however, the court declined to do so because petitioner was an out-of-State resident, and a support proceeding could be commenced by her in her home State under the Uniform Support of Dependents Law, thereby obviating the need for her to remain in New York. Accordingly, notwithstanding that the orders of filiation and support were the result of two distinct proceedings, the order of filiation is nonetheless reviewable upon an appeal from the support order, as it would be if the support order had been issued in the paternity proceeding; moreover, even if such review were not available, the Appellate Division would exercise its discretion to grant leave to appeal from the order of filiation (Family Ct Act, § 1112).

 

732 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


McMinn v. Oyster Bay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


105 A.D.2d 46; 482 N.Y.S.2d 773; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20677



December 17, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, dated August 10, 1981, modified, on the law, by (1) adding a declaration that the provision of section 1 of article 1 of the Building Zone Ordinance of the Town of Oyster Bay defining "family" is unconstitutional insofar as it prohibits two or more persons unrelated by blood, marriage or adoption to live together, (2) adding a provision that the ordinance is violative of the right of due process under section 6 of article I of the New York State Constitution, insofar as it prohibits occupancy of one-family homes by persons unrelated by blood, marriage or adoption, (3) adding a provision enjoining enforcement of the ordinance to the extent it is declared unconstitutional herein, and (4) adding a provision declaring that the ordinance is constitutional insofar [***2] as it limits occupancy of one-family homes to members of a single housekeeping unit. As so modified, judgment affirmed, with costs to the plaintiffs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), held invalid a building zone restriction of defendant town prohibiting occupancy of single-family dwellings by two persons unrelated to each other by blood, marriage, or adoption. Plaintiff property owners and the town cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The town enacted a zoning restriction that, with a limited exception for two persons 62 years of age or over, restricted occupancy of one-family homes to persons related by blood, marriage, or adoption. The property owners rented their home to four unrelated young men. The town building inspector instituted criminal proceedings against the property owners under the zoning restriction. The property owners and their tenants responded with a suit against the town, its supervisor, and building inspector. The lower court held that the restriction violated state equal protection rights and a state human rights law. In affirming the judgment on other grounds, the court held that (1) because the human rights law prohibited discrimination based on marital status and not occupancy by unrelated persons, it was inapplicable, and (2) the zoning restriction was fatally unconstitutional because it violated the due process clause of the N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6.


OUTCOME: The judgment holding the ordinance invalid, as modified, was affirmed. The court ordered that the judgment be modified by adding a declaration that the restriction was unconstitutional insofar as it prohibited two or more persons unrelated by blood, marriage, or adoption from living together, by adding a provision that it violated due process under the state constitution, and by enjoining enforcement of the restriction.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Constitutionality of Ordinance Restricting Occupancy of One-Family House

1. A town zoning ordinance which, with a limited exception for two persons 62 years of age and over, restricts occupancy of one-family homes to persons related by blood, marriage or adoption is so arbitrary and fatally overbroad that it violates the due process clause of the State Constitution (NY Const, art I, § 6). The instant ordinance exceeds the original purpose of "single-family" designations in zoning ordinances, i.e., to control types of housing and living and not the genetic or intimate internal family relations of human beings, and conclusively presumes that occupancy by persons unrelated by blood, marriage or adoption is inimical to the general welfare; while there is a legitimate local interest in preventing overcrowding and traffic congestion, restricting single household composition to related persons is not a means reasonably related to the ends sought since, in the absence of a presumption of investigation [***3] and knowledge by the municipal authorities and of evidence to support the municipality's position, the conclusive presumption that unrelated people, who join to share companionship and costs as a single housekeeping unit in one-family homes, disrupt their neighborhoods and create other problems cannot be accepted on the instant record.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Standing -- Mootness

2. In a declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of a zoning ordinance which, with a limited exception for two persons 62 years of age or over restricts occupancy of one-family homes to persons related by blood, marriage or adoption, the issues have not been mooted nor have plaintiffs been deprived of standing by either plaintiff owners' contract to sell the one-family house in question, or by their tenants' move to another one-family house within the municipality, where a criminal prosecution based on the ordinance is still pending, where plaintiff owners, who also own several other houses within the municipality, at least one of which is concededly subject to the ordinance, have asserted a claim that the ordinance has had a negative effect on the value of their [***4] property, and where the tenants remain in violation of the ordinance in their new home.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Discrimination Based on Marital Status -- Exclusionary Zoning

3. A zoning ordinance which, with a limited exception for two persons 62 years of age or over, restricts occupancy of one-family homes to persons related by blood, marriage or adoption, does not conflict with subdivision 5 of section 296 of the Executive Law, since a restriction against occupancy by persons who have no familial relationship does not constitute illegal discrimination based on "marital status" under the statute; moreover, the proof adduced at trial was insufficient to sustain a finding that the ordinance constituted exclusionary zoning, where plaintiffs failed to make the requisite factual demonstration as to regional needs or that the ordinance was enacted with an exclusionary purpose.

 

733 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Highway Tavern Corp. v. McLaughlin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


105 A.D.2d 122; 483 N.Y.S.2d 323; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20682



December 17, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Determination confirmed and proceeding dismissed on the merits insofar as it is to review said determination. Proceeding otherwise converted to a declaratory judgment action and it is declared that as applied herein subdivision 6 of section 106 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, and rule 36 (subd 1, par [r]) of the New York State Liquor Authority Rules (9 NYCRR 53.1 [r]), on its face, are constitutional.

No costs or disbursements are awarded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner alcohol licensee (licensee) sought to annul the judgment of respondent State Liquor Authority (state) that it violated N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 106(6) and N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 51.1(r). The action was transferred to the court from the Supreme Court, Suffolk County (New York) pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 7804(g).


OVERVIEW: The licensee sought a judgment, pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78, in order to annul the state's order, which had suspended its license for 80 days and ordered the forfeiture of a $ 1,000 bond. The state determined that the licensee had permitted the premises to become disorderly and permitted a female to appear in such a manner as to expose her pubic hair, vulva and/or anus. The licensee insisted that the action could not be sustained because N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 106, as applied, and the State Liquor Authority rule, on its face, unduly interfered with the freedom of expression as guaranteed by the constitution. The court disagreed, holding that the specific conduct lost its constitutional protections. Applying contemporary community standards, it appealed to prurient interest, depicted or described in a patently offensive way, sexual conduct specifically defined by applicable state law, and taken as a whole, it lacked serious artistic value. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment suspending the alcohol license for 80 days and ordering the forfeiture of the licensees' bond was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute

1. Subdivision 6 of section 106 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law which [***2] provides that "no person licensed to sell alcoholic beverages shall * * * suffer or permit [the licensed] premises to become disorderly", is constitutional as applied to the situation where petitioner licensee allowed a 30-minute lewd and indecent sexual performance to occur within its premises during which a nude female dancer exposed her vaginal and anal areas, self-manipulated her vagina and spread the cheeks of her buttocks. The primary purpose of the freedom of expression encompassed by the First and Fourteenth Amendments to the United States Constitution, and section 8 of article I of the New York Constitution is to encourage the free dissemination of ideas, and lewd and indecent performances in a bar licensed by the State to sell liquor for consumption on the premises is not a recognized form of protected expression.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Rule of State Liquor Authority -- Prohibiting Indecent Performances within Licensed Premises

2. Rule 36 of the Rules of the State Liquor Authority (9 NYCRR 53.1 [r] [2]) authorizing the revocation, cancellation, or suspension of a license to sell alcoholic beverages "[for] suffering or permitting on licensed [***3] premises * * * any person to appear unclothed or in such manner or attire as to expose to view any portion of the pubic hair, anus, vulva or genitals, or any simulation thereof", is a legitimate exercise of the State's power to regulate the sale of alcoholic beverages and therefore constitutional on its face pursuant to section 2 of the Twenty-first Amendment to the United States Constitution which curtails the operative scope of the First Amendment's protection of nude dancing. In prohibiting performances that partake more of gross sexuality than of communication, rule 36 (subd 1, par [r], cl [2]), on its face, does not interfere with State and Federal constitutional guarantees of freedom of expression (NY Const, art I, § 8; US Const, 1st, 14th Amdts).

Declaratory Judgments -- Conversion of Article 78 Proceeding to Declaratory Judgment Action

3. An article 78 proceeding brought by petitioner, a retail licensee for on-premises consumption of alcoholic beverages, seeking a judgment that the findings of respondent New York State Liquor Authority that petitioner violated subdivision 6 of section 106 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law and rule 36 (subd 1, par [r],  [***4] cl [2]) of the State Liquor Authority Rules (9 NYCRR 53.1 [r] [2]) by permitting its licensed premises to become disorderly and permitting a lewd and indecent performance to occur therein are arbitrary, whimsical and capricious, and/or nonsupported by fact, and unconstitutional, must be converted to an action for a declaratory judgment, since petitioner is challenging the constitutionality of the statute and the rule, as opposed to the action taken by respondent in suspending petitioner's license for 80 days pursuant to the aforesaid statute and rule.

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses -- Disorderly Conduct on Premises

4. Petitioner retail licensee for on-premises consumption of alcoholic beverages has violated subdivision 6 of section 106 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law which prohibits a licensee from suffering or permitting the licensed premises to become disorderly where a female dancer shouted obscenities for 10 to 15 minutes at the crowd during an altercation in an apparent effort to incite further fighting while she waved a pool cue and threw a pool ball into the crowd, with no attempt made by the manager to stop the disturbance. Although the incident was [***5] spontaneous and not foreseeable, the manager had actual knowledge of what was going on and permitted it to continue.

 

734 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Blau-Par Corp. v. New York State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


106 A.D.2d 503; 482 N.Y.S.2d 841; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21534



December 17, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a liquor license holder, appealed a decision by respondent, the New York State Liquor Authority, which suspended the holder's liquor license.


OVERVIEW: The Authority suspended the holder's liquor license after a hearing on the grounds that the holder allowed lewd and indecent activities, including nude dancing, in its establishment in violation of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 53.1(r)(1). The holder claimed that the determinations were not based on substantial evidence and that § 53.1(r)(1) was unconstitutional. The court held that the suspension was proper because it was supported by sufficient evidence. Because U.S. Const. amend. XXI allowed for states to regulate alcoholic beverages, the state had the authority to regulate the establishments that served alcohol. Therefore, the prohibitions under § 53.1(r)(1) were proper. N.Y. Const. art. I, § 8 was not violated by the prohibition of nude dancing, though it did prohibit bans on topless dancing in establishments that served liquor. The term "lewd and indecent" was not vague because it was synonymous with "obscene." Thus, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 53.1(r)(1) was specific enough to show what was prohibited. The Authority had the power to determine whether the performance in the holder's establishment was lewd and indecent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision against the holder.

People v. Carrisquello


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


106 A.D.2d 513; 483 N.Y.S.2d 47; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21550



December 17, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged two judgments from the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which convicted him of two counts of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree, upon his pleas of guilty, and sentenced him to concurrent indeterminate terms of imprisonment of four and one-half to nine years.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to two counts of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree. On appeal he contended that the pleas had to be vacated because he was not advised of his rights to testify in his own behalf and have the prosecution prove his guilt beyond a reasonable doubt to a unanimous jury. The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence of four and one-half to nine years imprisonment because he had failed to move to withdraw his plea on the cited ground prior to the imposition of sentence or to vacate the judgment under N.Y. Law § 470.05(2).The court found no merit in defendant's argument that his sentence was excessive because he received what he had bargained for, which was the minimum sentence under the law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree.

 

736 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Clark v. Cuomo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


104 A.D.2d 188; 483 N.Y.S.2d 828; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20612



December 13, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, on the law, without costs. Executive Order No. 43 declared to be constitutional, and defendants and the Voter Registration Task Force are enjoined from providing receptacles for completed voter registration forms at those agency locations where the forms are made available.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant governor and others sought review of a decision by the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which, on the application of respondent party chairman, declared an executive order regarding voter registration unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The governor issued an executive order, which established a program to encourage voter registration and created a "Voter Registration Task Force." The party chairman brought an action to have the executive order declared unconstitutional. The court at special term held that the executive order was unconstitutional and enjoined implementation of the governor's program. The governor appealed and the court reversed. The court found that the executive order did not violate the state constitution's mandate that voter registration be conducted in a bipartisan manner. The court said that order merely provided a means to make registration forms available to the public and that the party chairman offered no proof that the order violated the bipartisan mandate. The court also held that the order did not violate the separation of powers doctrine. The court noted that the state constitution charged the legislature with the duty of creating a voter registration system. The executive order did not infringe on that duty, the court said, because it did not create a new system of registration. Instead, the order merely used the existing system previously outlined by the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment that, on the application of the party chairman, declared the governor's executive order on voter registration unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Executive Order -- State Program for Voter Registration

1. Executive Order No. 43, which directs State agencies which have regular business contacts with the public to conduct voter registration projects to assist citizens of the State in registering to vote and creates a "Voter Registration Task Force" to develop training programs for State employees involved in the voter registration effort, does not violate section 8 of article II of the New York Constitution because it does not create a board "charged with the duty of registration". The order merely provides [***2] a means to make registration forms available to the public, and a person is not registered until the application form is reviewed and examined by at least two members of the county board of elections who shall represent the two major parties (Election Law, § 5-210, subd 6, par [a]).

Constitutional Law -- Separation of Powers -- Validity of Executive Order Establishing State Program for Voter Registration

2. Executive Order No. 43, which directs State agencies which have regular business contacts with the public to conduct voter registration projects to assist citizens of the State in registering to vote and creates a "Voter Registration Task Force" to develop training programs for State employees involved in the voter registration effort, is not unconstitutional as violating the separation of powers doctrine. The Legislature has explicitly declared a public policy in favor of the broadest voter participation in elections as well as the widest distribution possible of mail registration applications; the executive order does not create a new system of registration, which is clearly the responsibility of the Legislature (NY Const, art II, § 6), but merely uses the existing [***3] system outlined by the Legislature in the Election Law. Therefore, the executive order does not seek to create a State-wide program for voter registration and enrollment in political parties, nor does it infringe on any power explicitly delegated to the Legislature in the State Constitution.

Elections -- Registration -- Implementation of Executive Order Establishing State Program for Voter Registration

3. The provision of receptacles for completed voter registration forms at those State agency locations where the forms are made available under the authority of Executive Order No. 43 is enjoined; in carrying out the passive objective of the executive order of making voter registration forms more available and accessible to the public, State agency personnel should not handle completed forms; the completed forms should be mailed directly to the local board of elections. However, signs displayed at the various State agencies which are enlarged copies of mail registration forms and contain the notation "To vote in a primary election, you must register in a party" cannot be construed as uncontroverted evidence that assistance is offered in the enrollment process.

People v. Laninger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


106 A.D.2d 759; 484 N.Y.S.2d 178; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21689



December 13, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment from the County Court of Chemung County (New York), which convicted defendant of burglary in the second degree. Defendant challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02 under which he was sentenced.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a plea of guilty to the charge of burglary in the second degree and was sentenced to an indeterminate prison term of not less than two nor more than six years. Defendant alleged that the sentencing statute, § 70.02, was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court held that § 70.02 had already been held constitutional. Pursuant to § 70.02(3)(b), an indeterminate sentence could be imposed with a maximum of 4 1/2 years not to exceed 15 years. The court held that defendant's sentence was within the permissible statutory limit.

738 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Harris Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


104 A.D.2d 130; 483 N.Y.S.2d 442; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20221



November 29, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people challenged an order of the Clinton County Court (New York), which granted defendants' motion to dismiss the indictment, which charged defendants, a corporation and two non-officer employees, with three counts of unlawful dealing in hazardous wastes in the first degree, in violation of N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law §§ 71-2717(1), (3).


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the dismissal of the indictment because the regulations did not give proper notice of the prohibited conduct. In so ruling, the court held that N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 6, § 366.4, an important element of the alleged crimes, was void because it contravened N.Y. Const. art. IV, § 8 and the State Administrative Procedures Act. N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 6, § 366.4 attempted to list the "hazardous wastes" subject to N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law. § 71-2717. However, it was void as originally promulgated because it attempted to incorporate, by reference, various federal regulations listing hazardous wastes without filing the full text of the federal regulations so referenced, in violation of N.Y. Const. art. IV, § 8. Further, the subsequent filing of the full text of the federal regulations, without more, did not validate the regulation. Rather, to be constitutionally acceptable, compliance with the State Administrative Procedure Act was necessary, which involved a new notice and comment process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that dismissed the indictment charging defendants with three counts of unlawful dealing in hazardous wastes in the first degree.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation -- Hazardous Waste -- Unlawful Dealing in Hazardous Wastes

1. ECL 71-2717, which proscribes conduct constituting unlawful dealing in hazardous wastes in the first degree, is not unconstitutionally vague because several of its key terms, including the phrase "hazardous wastes" are not defined therein; the definitions contained in ECL 71-2702 pertain to ECL 71-2717, although the latter is not identified as one of the sections to which the definitions apply.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Hazardous Waste Regulations

2. An indictment charging defendants with unlawful dealing in hazardous wastes in the first degree in violation of ECL 71-2717 was properly dismissed, since 6 NYCRR 366.4, the regulation promulgated thereunder which defines hazardous wastes, is invalid; even assuming the adoption of Federal lists of hazardous wastes in the regulation as originally promulgated did not violate the prohibition against [***2] incorporation by reference in section 16 of article III of the New York Constitution, the failure of the Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) to file the full text of the Federal regulations listing hazardous wastes violates section 8 of article IV of the New York Constitution, which provides that no rule made by any State department or office shall be effective "until it is filed in the office of the department of state". Furthermore, subsequent filing by the DEC of the full text of the Federal regulations to be incorporated by reference as an appendix to 6 NYCRR 366.4 was not sufficient to validate the regulation, which would have required a new notice and comment process in accordance with the State Administrative Procedure Act; finally, 6 NYCRR 366.4 could not serve as a basis for the indictment in any event, since the initial filing of the proposed regulations was also ineffective, inasmuch as they were not filed with the Secretary of State within 180 days after the publication of the notice of proposed rule making or within 180 days of the date of the last public hearing thereon ( ECL 27-0903, subd 1; State Administrative Procedure Act, § 202, subd 1, par [e]; subd [***3] 2, par [d]; § 203).
