People v. McBride


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, First Department


105 A.D.2d 658; 482 N.Y.S.2d 3; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20766



November 27, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which convicted defendant, upon pleas of guilty, of one count of robbery in the second degree and one count of attempted robbery in the first degree, and sentenced him as a persistent violent felony offender to concurrent indeterminate terms of six years to life imprisonment pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 70.10 (1)(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to and was convicted of one count of robbery in the second degree and one count of attempted robbery in the first degree. He was sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender to concurrent indeterminate terms of six years to life imprisonment. Sentencing for the first violent felony occurred after the defendant had committed the second felony. Defendant appealed, claiming that enhanced sentences were unconstitutional and that he had been coerced into entering a guilty plea. The court found that according to N.Y. Penal Law § 70.10 (1)(c), the two felonies only counted as one conviction for the purposes of enhanced sentencing. Defendant's guilty plea was a voluntary choice. The court modified the judgment to reflect the correct sentence according to the statute and, as modified, affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment to reverse the adjudication of defendant as a persistent violent felony offender because the two felonies only counted as one conviction for purposes of enhanced sentencing. The court affirmed the remainder of the judgment.

 

740 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Costantakos v. Bd. of Educ.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


105 A.D.2d 825; 482 N.Y.S.2d 27; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20948



November 26, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teacher appealed from an order of the Supreme Court of Kings County (New York), which granted defendant New York City Board of Education's motion for summary judgment and dismissing the teacher's complaint, which challenged a by-law that had been promulgated by the Board as unconstitutional under the equal protection clause of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The teacher filed an action to declare part of a Board by-law unconstitutional under the equal protection clause of the United States Constitution. The trial court granted the Board's motion for summary judgment. The court reversed because the trial court erred in dismissing the complaint as time barred. A declaratory judgment action is normally governed by a six-year statute of limitations period. However, where the action could have been brought as a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, a four-month limitations period applies. A proceeding under art. 78 was not the proper alternate form of judicial proceeding that was open to the teacher because the proceeding was not available to challenge a legislative act. The Board's denial of the teacher's request for a salary credit on the basis of the by-law was a legislative act. The label applied because the dispute centered upon a rule of general applicability rather than an ad hoc determination of an individual party's particular rights. The teacher's case could have been brought as an art. 78 proceeding and, therefore, the six-year rather than the four-year limitations period applied, rendering the action timely.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court that granted the Board's motion for summary judgment in a declaratory action, which was brought by the teacher to declare a by-law that had been promulgated by the Board unconstitutional.

People v. Balls

Savemart, Inc. v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


105 A.D.2d 1001; 482 N.Y.S.2d 150; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21092



November 21, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers challenged the decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which sustained a determination of respondent State Tax Commission with respect to a sales tax assessment imposed under N.Y. Tax Law art. 28, 29.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers ran a large-volume retailer and wholesale distributor of electronic home entertainment equipment. The Commission determined that the sale of televisions to a third party was a retail sale subject to sales tax, and because the taxpayers failed to collect sales tax, they were liable for it. The taxpayers claimed that the record did not support the conclusion that the sale was a retail transaction. The court disagreed, holding that under N.Y. Tax Law §§ 1101(b)(4)(i), 1105(a), the disputed transaction fell within the definition of retail sales subject to sales tax. The purchaser did not deliver a certificate showing the purchase was for resale, so the taxpayers were liable for the tax under N.Y. Tax Law § 1132(c). The court also rejected the taxpayers' claim that the transaction was not a taxable event because the televisions were delivered to a carrier in New York but were destined for an out-of-state purchaser. The evidence demonstrated that the agreement for the sale of the televisions was finalized in New York, payments were received in New York and, most importantly, the taxpayers made arrangements with the carrier to pick up the televisions in New York.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the supreme court upholding the Commission's imposition of sales tax on the taxpayers.

 

744 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Kradjian v. Binghamton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


104 A.D.2d 16; 482 N.Y.S.2d 89; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20200



November 15, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order modified, on the law, without costs, by reversing so much thereof as dismissed the complaint of plaintiffs Diran Kradjian and Harry Kradjian and by adding thereto provisions declaring that neither subdivision 2 of section 99-h of the General Municipal Law nor ordinance 83-11 of the City of Binghamton violates section 1 of article VIII of the New York State Constitution and that any violation of the Open Meetings Law in enacting said ordinance does not require that the ordinance be declared null and void, and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, owners and hotel, challenged an order from the Broome County Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which granted a motion by defendant city for summary judgment dismissing the complaint, holding that plaintiffs lacked standing to mount a constitutional challenge. Plaintiffs contend that the complaint against the city should not have been dismissed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs commenced this action against the city and its local development corporation seeking, inter alia, a judgment declaring unconstitutional the state statute and the city ordinance authorizing participation in a federal loan program, under the Housing and Community Development Act of 1974 (Act), 42 U.S.C.S. § 5301 et seq., to assist in financing the construction of a downtown hotel. The court held that plaintiffs failed to overcome the strong presumption of constitutionality of state statutes. The court noted that plaintiffs failed to show an extension of municipal funds or credit to the private developers. The court found that the city's participation was intended to promote urban expansion and growth to attract investments and create permanent job opportunities for low and moderate income persons. The court saw no gift or loan of municipal funds in violation of the state constitution. The court opined that the federal statute did not require the city to guarantee the loan by pledging its full faith and credit, so the required security was limited to future federal grants under the Act and proceeds from foreclosure sale of the property. The order was affirmed as modified.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's order and affirmed as modified. The court found no infractions of the state constitution and dismissed the complaint of the hotel owners.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing

1. In an action seeking a declaration that subdivision 2 of section 99-h of the General Municipal Law and an ordinance of the City of Binghamton, which authorize the city's participation in a Federal loan program, are unconstitutional, it was improper to conclude that plaintiffs, residents and taxpayers in the city, lack standing on the basis of a finding that the city's [***2] participation in the loan program did not involve the expenditure of municipal funds or the extension of municipal credit; such a determination is more appropriately left for the merits, rather than in the context of the threshold issue of standing.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Participation of City in Federal Loan Program

2. Plaintiffs are not entitled to a judgment declaring unconstitutional subdivision 2 of section 99-h of the General Municipal Law and an ordinance of the City of Binghamton which authorize the city's participation in a Federal loan program pursuant to the Housing and Community Development Act of 1974 (US Code, tit 42, § 5301 et seq.), where the city intends to use the loan proceeds to purchase a hotel from private developers and then reconvey the hotel to them retaining a mortgage to be repaid on terms equivalent to the city's obligation on the Federal loan. Inasmuch as the program is created by Federal statute, administered by a Federal agency and funded by Federal sources and the city's participation therein is intended to promote urban expansion so as to attract investments and create permanent job opportunities, there is no [***3] gift or loan of municipal funds in violation of section 1 of article VIII of the New York Constitution; although the city is required to furnish certain collateral and other security it is not required to guarantee the loan by pledging its full faith and credit and, therefore, there is no unconstitutional extension of municipal credit; moreover, speculation that the city might incur losses if the hotel fails as a business is inadequate to support plaintiffs' constitutional challenges.

Public Officers -- Open Meetings Law

3. There was no abuse of discretion by Special Term in not vacating an ordinance which authorized the City of Binghamton to participate in a Federal loan program notwithstanding allegations that the ordinance was passed in violation of the Open Meetings Law ( Public Officers Law, § 100 et seq.).

 

745 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Top Tile Bldg. Supply Corp. v. New York State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


105 A.D.2d 936; 481 N.Y.S.2d 903; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21042



November 15, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporations challenged a decision of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Albany County (New York), which made a determination in a N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 proceeding that the corporations failed to exhaust administrative remedies in their challenge to sales tax assessments by respondent tax commission under N.Y. Tax Law § 1141(b).


OVERVIEW: The corporations, which were domestic companies with some community of interest by common management, filed an application in the nature of prohibition to enjoin the collection of sales tax. The corporations claimed that the underlying assessment was totally fictitious and that § 1141(b) was unconstitutional. The supreme court dismissed the action because the corporations failed to exhaust their administrative remedies, as required by N.Y. Tax Law §§ 1138, 1140. On appeal, the corporations again sought to argue the merits of their positions, but the court considered the merits only as they pertained to possible jurisdictional infirmities. The court rejected the corporations' claims that §§ 1138, 1140 did not constitute an exclusive remedy under the circumstances in the case. The court found that the tax commission's assessments were not made for the purpose of merely extending the limitations period, and further, the corporation had consented to an extension. The court held that tax commission's notices of determination were issued within the limitations period, and there was no remedy other than that in §§ 1138, 1140.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court's judgment.

 

746 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Howard Johnson Co. v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


105 A.D.2d 948; 481 N.Y.S.2d 909; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21047



November 15, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer obtained a refund for franchise taxes paid on investment income except for that portion of its investment income that represented its short-term notes. Respondent, the State Tax Commission (commission) sustained this assessment. The Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) dismissed the taxpayer's application to annul the commission's determination. The taxpayer sought review.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer claimed that the lower court erred in dismissing its petition. The court rejected the claim and affirmed the lower court's judgment. The court found that the term "other securities" in N.Y. Tax Law § 208(5) was patently ambiguous and that the commission's regulation interpreting this term was to be given effect. The court found that during the relevant tax years, the commission's regulation specifically excluded short-term notes from the term "other securities" in § 208(5). Although the regulation was later amended to include short-term notes in the definition of "other securities," the court held that the commission was bound by its regulation that was in effect during the relevant tax period. Accordingly, because the amendment that was relied upon by the taxpayer was promulgated after the tax years at issue, the court found that the commission's determination was correct and upheld the lower court's dismissal of the taxpayer's petition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that dismissed the taxpayer's petition.

 

747 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Foreclosure of Tax Liens


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


103 A.D.2d 636; 481 N.Y.S.2d 547; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20192



November 7, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged the order of the Erie County Court (New York), which granted a motion by appellee company to vacate and set aside an in rem judgment of foreclosure and sale.


OVERVIEW: The question presented on appeal was whether the Erie County Tax Act (the Act), which did not require personal notice to mortgagees prior to in rem tax foreclosure, was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, which set aside the tax foreclosure sale. The court found that the Act provided inadequate notice to satisfy due process requirements. The court noted that where a property interest was at stake, a party had to be afforded that degree of notice reasonably calculated, under all the circumstances, to apprise interested parties of the pendency of the action and afford them an opportunity to present their objections. The court determined that the Erie County statutes would only be exacerbated by the recent trend in which many noninstitutional lenders, particularly private persons who engaged in seller financing, financed real estate transactions. The court concluded that the state had a constitutional duty to provide notice to persons before divesting them of their property and that that constitutional duty could not be met by shifting the burden to the property owner.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which set aside the tax foreclosure sale.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Statute -- Notice to Mortgagee of In Rem Tax Foreclosure

The State has the constitutional obligation to provide notice to all mortgagees, not merely those who request notice, before divesting them of their interest in property through in rem tax foreclosure, and that duty may not be abrogated by requiring the mortgagee to request notice. Accordingly, the Erie County Tax Act (L 1942, ch 812, as amd) which does not require personal notice to mortgagees prior to in rem tax foreclosure but which contains a provision by which a mortgagee may request that the County Commissioner of Finance provide notice of an impending tax foreclosure is unconstitutional in that it provides inadequate notice to satisfy due process requirements.

Burkhart v. Ontario


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


105 A.D.2d 1056; 482 N.Y.S.2d 596; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21130



November 7, 1984 

 

749 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Foss v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Fourth Department


104 A.D.2d 99; 481 N.Y.S.2d 191; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20214



November 7, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Judgment unanimously affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner challenged a decision from the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York), which, in a declaratory judgment action, entered a judgment granting defendant city's motion for summary judgment and dismissed the amended complaint. The trial court also declared that N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 305 and art. 19 of the Real Property Tax Law were valid and constitutional, and denied the property owner's motion for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: In this declaratory judgment action the property owner challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 305, and art. 19 of the Real Property Tax Law and appealed from the grant of summary judgment to the city declaring the legislative enactments valid and constitutional. On appeal, the court held that § 305 was not void for vagueness for failure to mandate a specific fractional assessment so long as the fraction could be easily ascertained. The court held that there was no due process requirement that fractional assessment of property had to be uniform statewide. The court held that the classification of property as homestead and nonhomestead was permissible and that the legislature was free to delegate to local assessing units the power to impose heavier burdens on nonhomestead property than had existed historically. The court also held that in light of the state's traditional reliance on local assessing units to administer the property tax system, art. 19 did not deny any property owner equal protection of the law in the allocation of county taxes. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment of the supreme court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the supreme court, which had granted the city's motion for summary judgment, declared certain state real property tax law constitutional, and denied the real property owner's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Fractional Assessment

1. Section 305 of the Real Property Tax Law, which provides that "All real property in each assessing unit shall be assessed at a uniform percentage of value", is not unconstitutionally vague; "value" as used in the statute means market value. Furthermore, since all property within an assessment unit must be uniformly assessed at a common percentage of full value, which in the City of Rochester has been publicly declared to be 100%, the statute is reasonably definite on its face and, in any event, it is valid as applied to plaintiff, the owner of a four-family dwelling in that city; there is no due process requirement that fractional assessment [***2] must be uniform State-wide.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Homestead and Nonhomestead Property Classes

2. The statutory formula in article 19 of the Real Property Tax Law for establishing the homestead base proportion based on the assessment roll for the year prior to revaluation is not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power to local assessors. The Legislature did not delegate to local assessors the power to establish class tax rates; rather, it determined for itself to adopt the historical tax burdens for each class of homestead and nonhomestead property as reflected in the assessment roll as a starting point for the establishment of future tax rates, which decision had a rational basis wholly apart from the assessors' role in creating the de facto classification of property into residential and commercial classes which article 19 was enacted to preserve. In addition, article 19 delegates to local legislative bodies, not to the assessors, the discretion to adopt that article and, further, to adjust the proportion of taxes paid by each class.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Homestead and Nonhomestead Property Classes

3. Although the tax burden on properties [***3] of like class and value will not be uniform across the County of Monroe as a result of the adoption of article 19 of the Real Property Tax Law in the City of Rochester, article 19, which provides for the preservation of class shares of taxes for homestead and nonhomestead property, does not deny any property owner equal protection of the law in the allocation of county taxes, since all properties in a given class are treated uniformly within the assessing unit, which is the relevant jurisdiction. The legislative purpose in enacting article 19, i.e., to preserve the assessing unit as the primary political jurisdiction for the assessment of property and the allocation of property taxes, has a rational basis; the promotion of local control of taxation is a legitimate State interest and one to which the present system is reasonably related and, moreover, preserving the status quo by avoiding a sudden shift of the real property tax burden from commercial to residential property is a legitimate basis for legislative action and supports the validity of article 19.

 

750 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Haines v. Flacke


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


104 A.D.2d 26; 481 N.Y.S.2d 376; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20203



November 5, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Determination of the Commissioner of the Department of Environmental Conservation dated June 15, 1982, confirmed, on the merits, without costs or disbursements, and matter remitted to Special Term for an evidentiary hearing pursuant to Spears v Berle (48 NY2d 254, 261) to determine "whether the wetlands regulations, considered together with the denial of the [permits], would work an unconstitutional taking of petitioner's property."


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner tenant sought review of the determination of respondent Commissioner of the Department of Environmental Conservation (Commissioner), which denied an application by the tenant for a permit to place a single-family home and associated septic system on the subject property.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the tenant's lease of the subject property, he was entitled to erect and occupy a private one-family dwelling on said property. The property, however, was subject to the Tidal Wetlands Act, N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law art. 25. Thus, the tenant applied to the Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) for appropriate permits and any necessary variances to allow him to a dwelling and a septic tank system on the property. The application was denied, and the tenant sought review. The court confirmed the Commissioner's decision but remanded the case to the special term court for an evidentiary hearing to determine whether the regulations at issue constituted an unconstitutional taking of the tenant's property. The DEC's evidence provided substantial evidence for the denial of the applications. However, pursuant to prior case law on the issue, an evidentiary hearing was required in order to determine whether the DEC's denial of such permits constituted an unconstitutional taking of the tenant's property. Since this matter was transferred to the court under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, it did not have before it a proper evidentiary record on the confiscation issue.


OUTCOME: The court confirmed the denial of the tenant's application for permits. The court, however, remanded the case to the special term court for an evidentiary hearing on the issue of whether the denial constituted an unconstitutional taking of the tenant's property.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation -- Tidal Wetlands

1. A determination of the Commissioner of the Department of Environmental Conservation (DEC) denying petitioner's application for a permit to place a single-family home and septic system on an undeveloped lot leased from the town, which lot contains tidal wetlands and is subject [***2] to the provisions of ECL article 25 and 6 NYCRR 661.6, is confirmed, since said determination was rational and supported by substantial evidence, where the Commissioner adopted the administrative law judge's posthearing findings that the wetlands boundary line established by DEC staff, consistent with statutory criteria, must be considered as the proper boundary line on the site; that petitioner could not meet the 75-foot house setback requirement and the 100-foot septic system requirement of 6 NYCRR 661.6; that the variances needed would be "extraordinary"; that the proposed house would encroach some 40 feet into high marsh area and would thus require a 115-foot variance to the 75-foot setback requirement; that the existing houses within 500 feet of the site are setback an average of 15 feet from the wetlands, and that the proposed sanitary system would have a zero setback from the tidal wetlands boundary and would thus require a 100-foot variance to the 100-foot septic system setback requirement.

Environmental Conservation -- Tidal Wetlands

2. Although the Appellate Division confirmed a determination of the Commissioner of the Department of Environmental Conservation [***3] denying petitioner's application for a permit to place a single-family home and septic system on an undeveloped lot leased from the town, which lot contains tidal wetlands and is subject to ECL article 25 and 6 NYCRR 661.6, petitioner is entitled to a hearing on the issue of whether said denial constitutes the equivalent of a taking without compensation within the meaning of ECL 25-0404 as would require the Commissioner to acquire the wetlands or such rights in them as have been taken, proceeding under the power of eminent domain; accordingly, the matter is remitted to Special Term for an evidentiary hearing on said issue. In future proceedings authorized by ECL 25-0404, absent unusual circumstances, Special Term should determine, in the first instance, whether the denial of the permit should be sustained, and if the answer to that question is affirmative, whether a confiscation hearing is warranted; it would then hold such a hearing and render a determination on the confiscation question and, should there be an appeal, the appellate court would have the benefit of those initial findings and determinations.

 

751 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Amsterdam Brush Corp. v. Amsterdam


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


105 A.D.2d 881; 482 N.Y.S.2d 352; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 21009



November 1, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant City of Amsterdam appealed the order of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Montgomery County (New York), which denied the city's motion to dismiss plaintiff brush corporation's suit for negligence.


OVERVIEW: The brush company owned a factory within the city. A city creek that originated from a city reservoir flowed onto the brush company's property and under the factory. After a heavy rainfall, the brush company's property was damaged when debris in the creek, which had accumulated due to the city's negligence, formed a natural dam under the factory and caused flooding. The brush company sued the city. The city moved to dismiss for failure to state a claim and on the basis that the brush company had failed to comply with the city charter that required prior notice of a dangerous or defective condition. The trial court denied the motion to dismiss and the city appealed. The court found that the city's negligence occurred in places upstream that were not enumerated in the city charter or addressed by statute, thus, prior notice was not required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court, which denied the city's motion to dismiss, and awarded costs to the brush company.

753 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brabham


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


104 A.D.2d 1043; 481 N.Y.S.2d 111; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20479



October 29, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which convicted him of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree, upon his plea of guilty, and sentenced him as a persistent violent felony offender to an indeterminate term of six years to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 70.02 (1)(c) and sentenced as a persistent violent felony offender to an indeterminate term of six years to life imprisonment. On appeal, defendant claimed that the mandatory sentencing scheme of the persistent violent offender statute was unconstitutional and that a six year to life prison sentence was so disproportionate and inappropriate to the crime for which he was convicted that the sentence constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The court affirmed. The persistent violent felony offender statutes were facially constitutional. Defendant admitted to walking the streets with a loaded gun; the legislature's requirement that such an offense have a severe and inflexible punishment was not irrational.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence under the violent felony offender statute because the statute was not unconstitutional and the mandatory minimum prison term was not irrational.

 

754 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ardor Management Corp. v. Division of Housing & Community Renewal


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


104 A.D.2d 984; 480 N.Y.S.2d 903; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20419



October 22, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlord sought review of the decision from the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which dismissed its petition for review of the determination of respondent state agency that determined that the landlord's building was regulated under the Emergency Tenant Protection Act (Act) and that the landlord had charged a tenant excessive rent.


OVERVIEW: A tenant filed a complaint against the landlord with the state agency and the state agency determined that the landlord's property was subject to the Act because it was built prior to 1974 and that the landlord had charged the tenant excessive rent. The landlord filed for reconsideration and asserted that the Act did not apply to his building and that the Act was unconstitutional. The state agency affirmed its order and the landlord filed an action to set aside the state agency's determination. The trial court dismissed the petition to the extent that it claimed the Act was unconstitutional and that the Act was not applicable to the landlord's property. The landlord challenged the decision and the court affirmed. The evidence of record showed that the building was occupied prior to 1974 and that temporary certificates of occupancy had been issued. The building was completed within the meaning of the Act because it was substantially finished and the Act did not require a permanent certificate of occupancy to determine the date of completion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the landlord's challenge to the state agency's decision that the Act applied to the landlord's property.

756 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brown


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


105 A.D.2d 509; 481 N.Y.S.2d 771; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20543



October 18, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the County Court of Chemung County (New York), which convicted and sentenced defendant on two counts of criminal sale of a controlled substance in the third degree.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to entry of defendant's guilty plea and imposition of punishment, defendant argued that the sentence imposed was unduly harsh and excessive and that the imposition of a mandatory surcharge was unconstitutional. On review, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. In reaching its conclusion, the court held that defendant was not entitled to the punishment of lifetime probation that he sought, despite his cooperation with authorities, because prosecution did not recommend such a sentence and the plea agreement specified a sentence with a minimum of three years in prison. The court then rejected defendant's equal protection challenge to the mandatory surcharge, relying on its prior caselaw that upheld the constitutionality of the statute related to the surcharge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

 

757 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Handy v. Westbury Teachers Asso.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


104 A.D.2d 923; 480 N.Y.S.2d 728; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 20372



October 15, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, various employees of the education system, sought review of a decision from the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which directed defendants, the Board of Education and the American Federation of Teachers and other affiliated organizations, to submit to plaintiffs a detailed statement setting forth certain of plaintiffs' agency shop fees, and dismissed causes of action asserted on both sides.


OVERVIEW: In an action for an injunction, plaintiffs appealed from so much of a judgment that defendants were directed to submit to plaintiffs a detailed statement setting forth the expenditure of plaintiffs' agency shop fees for certain years, clearly delineating plaintiffs' pro rata share of expenditures, and that the judgment dismissed their complaint in all other respects. Defendants cross-appealed the dismissal of the balance of plaintiffs' complaint, directing them to submit said statement to plaintiffs, inter alia. Upon review, the court modified the order in several specific matters, and remitted many matters to the prior court for determinations according to its opinion. The court held that it was error to treat defendants' motions to dismiss plaintiffs' complaint as motions for summary judgment without affording notice to the parties of its intention to do so, and that several of plaintiffs' causes of action, challenging the constitutionality of laws that permits union defendants to use their agency shop fees for purposes other than collective bargaining, contract administration and grievance adjustment were actions restored and remitted for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order and reversed judgment, finding that it was error to dismiss many of the causes of action alleged in the complaints of both plaintiffs and defendants. The court listed those modifications to the order and restorations of the causes of action to be restored and remitted for further proceedings.

 

758 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Consolidated Edison Co. v. State Bd. of Equalization & Assessment


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


103 A.D.2d 453; 480 N.Y.S.2d 789; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19770



October 11, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with one bill of costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant special franchise holder filed against respondent State Board of Equalization and Assessment (SBEA) in the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York) a challenge to the SBEA's tax assessment as violative of due process and equal protection. The trial court granted the SBEA partial summary judgment as to the special franchise holder's challenge to the equalization rate. The special franchise holder appealed.


OVERVIEW: A special franchise was a right to maintain systems that transported, through public areas, water, steam, gas, or electricity. The SBEA taxed special franchise property using an equalization rate. By a 1982 amendment, N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 744(1) (1982) provided that the equalization rate was the sole evidence of equality with regard to assessment. As to due process, because special franchises were different from other real property, the state was permitted to enact the amendment as a procedural means to ensure equal ratio assessment between special franchise property and other property. As to equal protection, the amendment was rationally related to preserving equality and uniformity in taxation. Retroactive application of the amendment did not deprive persons of rights that earlier existed under the statute. The court's ruling prior to the amendment that § 744 allowed a challenge to the equalization rate was only an interpretation of § 744 that was determined incorrect by the amendment. Article 7 of the Real Property Tax Law was the exclusive procedure for reviewing real property tax assessment. N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 was unavailable to challenge the equalization rates.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's orders which denied the special franchise holder's challenges to the SBEA's real property tax assessment as violative of due process and equal protection.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Assessment -- Challenge of Equalization Rate by Special Franchise Holder

1. Subdivision 1 of section 744 of the Real Property Tax Law, which precludes a special franchise holder from judicially contesting equalization rates, is constitutional; the Real Property Tax Law permits administrative review of tentative special franchise assessments and thus the administrative contesting of the equalization rate, and allows a challenge to the equalization rate in the event that the incorrect equalization rate or one which is not the latest rate has been applied. Since the Legislature has [***2] determined that the best procedural means by which its policy of equal ratio assessment between special franchises and other real property can be effectuated is by strict reliance on the equalization rate, and has not eliminated petitioner's right to judicial review generally, petitioner's due process rights have not been violated; subdivision 1 of section 744 is rationally related to the objective of equality and uniformity of taxation and there is no transgression of petitioner's equal protection rights. The fact that the equalization rates will be exclusive evidence of the issue of equality with regard to assessments of special franchises does not amount to a delegation of legislative power, and the State Board of Equalization and Assessment has not been delegated any power to set tax rates; there is no violation of section 2 of article XVI of the New York Constitution because the statute does not mandate that any particular equalization rate be set, and if the application of an equalization rate results in an assessment in excess of full value, the taxpayer could raise the point by way of an overvaluation cause of action.

Statutes -- Retroactive Application of Statute

 [***3] 2. The Legislature's action in making the amendment to subdivision 1 of section 744 of the Real Property Tax Law, which precludes a special franchise holder from judicially contesting equalization rates, retroactive in order to supersede a prior decision of the Appellate Division, as affirmed by the Court of Appeals, did not violate the doctrine of separation of powers; the amendment did not deprive petitioner of any cause of action or substantive right granted to it by the Appellate Division since there had not yet been any determination that the equalization rates were inaccurate and the court simply held that petitioner had a right to contest the equalization rates as a matter of statutory interpretation. Courts are obligated to apply retroactive modifications of the laws involved in pending litigation, and no final determination had been made at the time the Legislature amended the statute.

Taxation -- Assessment -- Judicial Review

3. Article 7 of the Real Property Tax Law is the exclusive procedure for review of real property assessments unless otherwise provided by law, and no other provision in the Real Property Tax Law gives a special franchise holder the right [***4] to review equalization rates by way of a CPLR article 78 proceeding; thus, petitioner cannot use an article 78 proceeding to challenge the accuracy of the equalization rates. Although petitioner's application to declare the 1982 amendment to subdivision 1 of section 744 of the Real Property Tax Law precluding a special franchise holder from judicially contesting equalization rates unconstitutional could have been the subject of a separate article 78 proceeding, petitioner chose to make such claim the subject of a cross motion in a proceeding pursuant to article 7 and has had a full opportunity to litigate its contentions in this regard; thus, no purpose would have been served by converting the proceeding into a CPLR article 78 proceeding.

 

759 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bratton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


103 A.D.2d 368; 480 N.Y.S.2d 324; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19758



September 24, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order of the County Court, Westchester County, entered March 23, 1983, reversed, on the law, that branch of defendant's pretrial motion which sought dismissal of the indictment on the ground that he had been denied a speedy trial denied, indictment reinstated, and matter remitted to the County Court, Westchester County, for further proceedings on the indictment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review of a order of the Westchester County Court (New York), which granted a motion by defendant to dismiss an indictment for first-degree robbery on the ground that defendant had been denied a speedy trial.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted a motion by defendant to dismiss an indictment for first-degree robbery on the ground that defendant had been denied a speedy trial. On appeal, the prosecution contended that the period of defendant's absence could not be charged to the prosecution because it was not the prosecution's policy to indict absent defendants. The court reversed the trial court's order of dismissal and reinstated the indictment. The court held that: (1) trial court erred in charging prosecution with the nearly 18-month period of defendant's absence; (2) a prosecutor who established a systematic non-indictment policy that sought to husband resources for timely trials of available defendants rather than for potentially unconstitutional trials of those who might never be apprehended was not chargeable with unjustified delay or neglect when a defendant's absence stalled his trial; (3) once due diligence had been exercised in seeking to locate a missing defendant in a jurisdiction that adhered to a non-indictment policy, it was clear that the defendant's absence was beyond the prosecution's control and the prosecution could not be charged with it.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, which granted defendant's motion to dismiss and indictment for first-degree robbery, and reinstated the indictment.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Right to Speedy Trial

An order granting a motion to dismiss an indictment, by a defendant who was apprehended nearly 18 months after an accusatory instrument had been filed against him, on the ground that he had been denied a speedy trial is reversed; the People may not be charged with the period of delay resulting from the absence or unavailability of the defendant ( CPL 30.30, subd 4, par [c]), and a prosecutor's policy of not indicting absent defendants accords with legislative intent without infringing on the rights of those who are not present; once due diligence has been exercised [***2] in seeking to locate a missing defendant in a jurisdiction that adheres to a nonindictment policy, defendant's absence is beyond the People's control and the People may not be charged with the period of the absence.

 

760 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bay Ridge Community Council, Inc. v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


103 A.D.2d 280; 479 N.Y.S.2d 746; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19273



September 17, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Resettled order and judgment (one paper) of the Supreme Court, Kings County, dated December 15, 1982, affirmed insofar as appealed from, without costs or disbursements.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant council challenged a resettled order and judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which declared, in favor of respondent governor, that the reapportionment plan of 1982 N.Y. Laws 455 was valid and constitutional.


OVERVIEW: A community council filed an action against the governor challenging the constitutionality of 1982 N.Y. Laws 455, which apportioned and distributed the state senate and assembly districts. The council appealed the declaration that the law was valid. The court affirmed because 1982 N.Y. Laws 455 complied with N.Y. Const. art. III, § 5. The contiguous districts with connected territory were compact and contiguous because they were subject to the equality of population requirement and were intended to maintain the voting power of minority groups in a county that lost districts by the census and to avoid a total population deviation in excess of 10 percent. The law did not invidiously discriminate against a political group or impermissibly impair voting powers in violation of the Equal Protection Clause, which prevailed over the inconsistent provisions of the state constitution requiring the joining of certain counties. The court held that the council's claim that 1982 N.Y. Laws 455 was a partisan gerrymander was non-justiciable and the council failed to submit sufficient evidence of partisan intent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in the governor's favor in the council's action for declaratory judgment that a reapportionment statute was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Legislature -- Reapportionment -- Constitutionality

Chapter 455 of the Laws of 1982, as amended, apportioning and distributing the Senate and Assembly districts of the State, complies with those provisions of article III of the New York Constitution which remain in force and effect and is otherwise valid as a matter of law. The law does not violate section 5 of article III, which directs that the counties of the State shall be divided "into assembly districts as nearly equal in number of inhabitants * * * of convenient and contiguous territory in as compact form as practicable"; there is no evidence that the law invidiously discriminates against any political group and [***2] there is thus no violation of the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution; the claim that the voting power of the voters of a particular New York City community has been impermissibly impaired cannot be sustained; the claim that the law amounts to a partisan political gerrymander is nonjusticiable; Hamilton and Fulton Counties are not required by section 5 of article III of the New York Constitution to be in the same Assembly district although that provision still remains in section 5 of article III, since it is part of the provision that stated that each county, regardless of population, was entitled to at least one Assembly district, and which was invalidated by the United States Supreme Court; the provision affording local legislatures in counties and cities entitled to more than one Assembly district the power to draw Assembly district lines has similarly been invalidated; and finally, numerous minor counties are not divided contrary to constitutional mandate.

 

761 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Glenwood TV, Inc. v. Ratner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


103 A.D.2d 322; 480 N.Y.S.2d 98; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19281



September 17, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Two judgments of the Supreme Court, Kings County, both entered July 20, 1981, reversed, on the law, with one bill of costs, determinations of the Department of Consumer Affairs confirmed, and proceedings dismissed on the merits, and it is declared that subdivision 5 of section B32-472.0 and section 773-13.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York are constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant New York City Department of Consumer Affairs appealed from the judgment of the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which declared New York City, New York, Rules of the City of New York, ch. 32, tit. B, art. 44, § B32-472.0, subdiv. 5 and § 773-13.0 unconstitutional insofar as they authorized nonconsensual periodic inspections of the records of licensed television and radio repair businesses without a search warrant.


OVERVIEW: An inspector from defendant arrived at the premises of plaintiff TV repair shops. The inspector asked plaintiffs for a sampling of the estimates, work orders, and final bills that they were required to keep and was refused. Administrative charges were filed against plaintiffs, a hearing was held, and compliance orders were rendered against plaintiffs. Plaintiffs refused to comply with the orders, resulting in the suspension and subsequent revocation of their licenses. Plaintiffs appealed, claiming that the inspections violated their U.S. Const. amend. IV protections against warrantless searches and seizures and the agency agreed. Defendant appealed and the court reversed, holding that an ex parte warrant or subpoena was not a necessary condition precedent to an administrative inspection. The court held that the administrative procedures provided more protection than a traditional search warrant, which was executed under the compulsion of the threat of force because if plaintiffs failed to comply, defendant's only recourse was to administrative and judicial process, providing plaintiffs with an adequate forum for judicial review.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the holdings of the lower court.


HEADNOTES

Administrative Law -- Warrantless Inspection of Business Records

1. Judgments of the Supreme Court declaring subdivision 5 of section B32-472.0 and section 773-13.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York unconstitutional insofar as they purport to authorize nonconsensual periodic inspections of the records of licensed television and radio repair businesses without [***2] a search warrant, and annulling determinations suspending petitioners' licenses and imposing fines for a willful refusal to permit such inspections, are reversed; the warrant clause is inapplicable to required records which are in the public domain and their custodian is not afforded the traditional protections of the Fourth Amendment; the Fourth Amendment is satisfied if the authority to inspect is exercised reasonably and if judicial review is available before a person is ultimately required to submit to an inspection. Inasmuch as the inspections were limited to required records and the licensees' public areas, the statutory safeguards were sufficient to protect their legitimate privacy concerns. The certainty and regularity of the scheme's application is sufficiently tailored to goals and enforcement needs and thus provides a constitutionally adequate substitute for a warrant, and there is an adequate forum for the licensee to obtain judicial review of any constitutional claims prior to enforcement.

Administrative Law -- Judicial Review -- Failure to Exhaust Administrative Remedies

2. While petitioners' failure to exhaust administrative remedies does not bar a challenge [***3] to administrative action as either unconstitutional or beyond the agency's statutory power, it limits the scope of judicial review to the face of the statute itself, and accordingly, in proceedings challenging those portions of the Administrative Code of the City of New York authorizing nonconsensual periodic inspections of the records of licensed television and radio repair businesses without a search warrant, it will be presumed that the inspections were conducted in a reasonable fashion and the findings of the hearing officer will be accepted.

 

762 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Clark v. Cuomo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


103 A.D.2d 244; 480 N.Y.S.2d 61; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19271



September 7, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, on the law, without costs, and plaintiff's motion denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, governor and state agencies, appealed an order from the Supreme Court at Special Term in Albany County (New York), which granted plaintiff Chairman of Republican state committee's motion for a preliminary injunction enjoining defendants from taking any action to enforce or implement Exec. Order No. 43 issued July 9, 1984.


OVERVIEW: Specifically, Exec. Order No. 43 directed defendants to make mail registrations forms available and to make staff members available to assist the public in completing the forms. The chairman filed suit, seeking to permanently enjoin implementation of the executive order on the grounds that it was unconstitutional and illegal. Subsequently, the chairman filed a motion for a preliminary injunction. The special term granted the motion. Thereafter, defendants filed an appeal, thereby effecting an automatic stay of the special term's order. On appeal, the court held that the special term abused its discretion in finding that the chairman demonstrated that he would suffer irreparable harm if defendants were not preliminary enjoined. In order to be entitled to a preliminary injunction, the chairman needed to show a likelihood of success on the merits, irreparable injury to him if the relief was not granted, and a balancing of the equities in his favor. Thus, the court found that the chairman failed to demonstrate immediate injury. Because the chairman failed to establish irreparable injury, the court declined to decide the remaining two prerequisites for a preliminary injunction.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the special term abused its discretion in finding that the chairman demonstrated that he would suffer irreparable harm if defendants were not preliminarily enjoined from taking any action to enforce or implement an executive order.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- Registration -- Authority of Governor to Establish State Program for Voter Registration

Due to his failure to show the requisite immediate irreparable harm, plaintiff Chairman of the New York Republican State Committee is not entitled to a preliminary injunction enjoining implementation of an executive order in which the Governor directed State agencies that have regular contact with the public, including the Departments of State, Labor, Motor Vehicles and Social Services and the Workers' Compensation Board, to conduct voter registration projects to assist citizens in registering to vote; the issue of the harm resulting from the initiation of the voter registration program by the executive rather than by the legislative branch of government must await [***2] resolution of the action following trial.

 

763 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Chase Manhattan Bank, N. A. v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


103 A.D.2d 211; 479 N.Y.S.2d 983; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19267



August 27, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Claims, dated June 2, 1982, reversed, on the law, without costs or disbursements, and matter remitted to the Court of Claims for further proceedings in accordance with the Per Curiam opinion herewith.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged an order from the Court of Claims (New York) that awarded judgment to respondent, trustee for deceased landowner (landowner), and valued the landowner's property taken in condemnation by the state as if the legal restrictions of the New York State Tidal Wetlands Act (Tidal Wetlands Act), N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 25, did not apply.


OVERVIEW: The landowner's property was appropriated for environmental purposes pursuant to N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 3-0305, and in the landowner's action to recover the property's value, the trial court valued the property as if the legal restrictions of the Tidal Wetlands Act did not apply. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that the trial court erred in valuing the property as if the Tidal Wetlands Act had been successfully directly attacked prior to the date of taking. The trial court should have valued the property as restricted by the Tidal Wetlands Act, with an increment for the reasonable probability of a successful judicial challenge to the statute's application as confiscatory. The court concluded that the probability-incremental increase rule applied; thus, the property should have been valued as if the Tidal Wetlands Act was in effect with an incremental increase representing the premium that a knowledgeable buyer would have paid for a potential change to a more valuable use. The court disagreed with the state's attempt to preclude any consideration of confiscation after the landowner failed to apply for a permit as the exultation of form over substance.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that valued the landowner's property as if the legal restrictions of the Tidal Wetlands Act did not apply. The case was remanded for a new hearing limited solely to the amount of any increment above the restricted recreational value that a knowledgeable buyer would have paid in light of the reasonable probability of successful constitutional court challenge.


HEADNOTES

Eminent Domain -- Compensation -- Tidal Wetlands

Classified wetlands taken in condemnation are to be valued as restricted by the Tidal Wetlands Act ( ECL 25-0101 et seq.) with an increment for the reasonable probability of a successful judicial challenge to the act's application as confiscatory; compliance with the permit and review procedures of the act is not a condition precedent to a confiscation determination in the condemnation proceeding, and the reasonable probability-incremental increase rule is applicable where the State is both the condemnor and regulatory authority; there is sufficient proof as to the economic return that could be realized under the permitted recreational use, the Court of Claims was justified in adopting claimant's comparable sales and rejecting the State's comparables, and its finding of feasibility [***2] of residential development of the parcel was not contrary to the evidence. Claimant carried its burden of proving that a constitutional challenge to the act as applied to the subject property would have, at least, a reasonable probability of success since the effect of the act is to deprive claimant of all financially rewarding uses of its property and to reduce the property's value by approximately 86%; the award of the full unrestricted residential value is vacated and the matter remitted for a new hearing limited solely to the amount of any increment, above the restricted recreational value, which a knowledgeable buyer would pay in light of the reasonable probability of a successful constitutional court challenge.

 

764 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Colwell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


103 A.D.2d 169; 479 N.Y.S.2d 904; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19259



August 23, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Broome County Court (New York), entering judgment of conviction of rape in the first degree and sodomy in the first degree, and resentencing defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was under suspicion of having committed sexual crimes. The arresting officer learned that defendant had been previously charged with burglary. The record, however, did not reflect that he had been convicted and sentenced and was still represented by counsel upon an appeal. After giving defendant Miranda warnings, which were waived, the police took his oral and written confessions that he committed rape and sodomy. The lower court convicted defendant. On appeal, the court affirmed. The burglary appeal was not a criminal action because the burglary conviction and sentence had terminated the action, under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 1.20(16). Because the action had terminated, his waiver of the right to counsel outside of the presence of counsel was effective. The failure of the police to ask about representation was not fatal to the waiver.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of conviction; defendant's waiver of counsel outside of the presence of counsel was constitutional because the appeal of his prior conviction was not considered a criminal action.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Right to Counsel

1. A judgment convicting defendant, upon his plea of guilty, of rape and sodomy is affirmed; while the police were aware that defendant had been charged in an unrelated burglary matter, the records did not indicate any disposition of the charge although in fact defendant had pleaded guilty to the charge and an appeal, in connection with which defendant was represented by counsel, was pending; the failure of the police to inquire into defendant's representation in regard to his appeal did not preclude his waiver of counsel when he received his Miranda warnings, and defendant's statements were thus not taken from him in violation of his right to counsel; an appeal and the attorney assigned to prosecute it are not part of a pending criminal action or charge.

Crimes -- Sentence

2. A defendant who pleaded [***2] guilty to rape and sodomy and negotiated a plea agreement whereby he would receive 7 1/2 to 15 years for each crime, the sentences to run concurrently, if a prior burglary conviction that was then on appeal was affirmed and 7 1/2 to 22 1/2 years if the burglary conviction were reversed, was properly resentenced to 7 1/2 to 22 1/2 years on each charge after the judgment of conviction for the burglary was reversed; the sentence originally imposed was the agreed-upon term for defendant as a second felony offender, and when the predicate felony conviction was reversed, the court had inherent power to vacate the sentence and impose a sentence upon defendant as a first offender. The sentence imposed was within the statutory limits and defendant was fully apprised before his plea of what his sentence would be if his appeal were successful; having voluntarily accepted a sentence less than the statutory maximum, defendant cannot now complain that his sentence was illegal or unduly severe.

 

765 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Allen v. North Hempstead


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


103 A.D.2d 144; 478 N.Y.S.2d 919; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19256



August 13, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Supreme Court, Nassau County, entered November 23, 1983, affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town challenged a judgment by the Supreme Court, Nassau County (New York), which declared its zoning ordinance requiring those seniors who wanted to purchase a condominium in a senior citizens residence district to first reside in the town for at least one year.


OVERVIEW: The local ordinance required residency in the town for at least one year before senior citizens could purchase a condominium in the newly developed senior residence district. A nonresident citizen challenged the ordinance. The trial court declared the durational residency requirement invalid and unconstitutional on equal protection grounds. On appeal, the court agreed that the requirement was invalid and unconstitutional, but on different grounds. The court held that the local zoning ordinance was invalid because it placed an impermissible restraint upon the users or owners of the property and because it also had an exclusionary purpose and impact. The court found no rational relationship among the durational residency requirement and the goal of providing appropriate and affordable housing for senior citizens.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's finding that the zoning ordinance was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Durational Residency Requirement

Section 70-89 (subd B) of article X of chapter 70 of the Code of the Town of North Hempstead, which requires that senior citizens must have legally resided in the town for at least one year in order to apply to live in a "Golden Age Residence District" is invalid as an impermissible restriction on the users or owners of property, which is particularly true in the instant situation since the ordinance creating these districts was based upon an implicit finding that appropriate, affordable housing for the elderly was not generally available in the Town of North Hempstead; therefore, plaintiffs and other similarly situated nonresident senior citizens are effectively precluded from moving to the Town of North [***2] Hempstead altogether, if they are precluded from obtaining housing in a "Golden Age Residence District". Further, said one-year durational residency requirement reflects an exclusionary purpose and has an exclusionary impact when coupled with a failure to consider the housing needs of senior citizens in the surrounding region, which are at least as great as those within the town. Accordingly, a judgment declaring said ordinance invalid and unconstitutional is affirmed.

Kent's Lounge, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Second Department


104 A.D.2d 397; 478 N.Y.S.2d 928; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 19859



August 13, 1984 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner lessee appealed a judgment of the Supreme Court, Richmond County (New York), which confirmed the determination of respondent Board of Standards and Appeals of the City of New York denial of the lessee's application to amend the certificate of occupancy to permit recreational dancing, and which declared that New York City Zoning Resolution § 32-15 was unconstitutionally invalid.


OVERVIEW: The lessee occupied premises in an area zoned to allow eating or drinking places without recreational dancing pursuant to New York City Zoning Resolution §§ 32-15, 32-21. The lessee filed for an amendment of the premises' certificate of occupancy, was denied by the Board, and unsuccessfully challenged that decision before the lower court. The lessee appealed, arguing that recreational dancing was a form of speech protected by the First Amendment to the United States Constitution and that the New York City Zoning Resolution's prohibition of recreational dancing infringed upon freedom of speech. The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that recreational dancing was not a form of speech protected by the First Amendment to the United States Constitution. The First Amendment protected dancing of a performer, paid or otherwise, presented for the entertainment of the public because it contained some element of communicating or advancing ideas, the court explained. Simple physical activity did not fall within the ambit of the First Amendment, the court said.


OUTCOME: The court found for the Board and affirmed the judgment of the lower court, without costs or disbursements.

 

767 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


New York State Coalition for Criminal Justice, Inc. v. Coughlin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of New York, Appellate Division, Third Department


103 A.D.2d 40; 479 N.Y.S.2d 850; 1984 N.Y. App. Div. LEXIS 18847



July 26, 1984 



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, special interest groups, taxpayers, and citizens, sought review of a judgment from the Supreme Court, Albany County (New York), which granted respondents', commissioner of correctional services and associated individuals, motion for summary judgment in a case where appellants sought to enjoin the issuance of bonds for prison construction.


OVERVIEW: Appellants sought to permanently enjoin respondents from issuing bonds for prison construction and argued that the Prison Construction Act, 1983 N.Y. Laws 56, was unconstitutional. The lower court granted respondents' motion for summary judgment and appellants sought review. The court affirmed the lower court judgment because it found that the Act did not violate the state constitution because no indemnity had been undertaken with respect to the bonds. The court concluded that the debts were necessary for the ongoing business of state government and were not subject to being challenged under N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 11.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court judgment that granted respondents' motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing

1. Plaintiffs, special interest groups and individual citizens and taxpayers, do not have standing as citizen-taxpayers under subdivision 1 of section 123-b of the State Finance Law or as taxpayers under a common-law right to bring an action to permanently enjoin defendants from issuing bonds for prison construction (see Prison Construction Act, L 1983, ch 56). A citizen-taxpayer does not have standing to challenge the constitutionality of the issuance of State bond anticipation notes under subdivision 1 of section 123-b, which grants a citizen-taxpayer standing with respect to State expenditures but does not apply to revenue raising; there is a clear legislative intent to prevent taxpayer challenges in such cases.

Parties -- Standing

2. In an action brought by special interest groups and individual citizens and taxpayers to permanently enjoin defendants from issuing bonds [***2] for prison construction (see Prison Construction Act, L 1983, ch 56), the Appellate Division will not address plaintiffs' contention that they have standing as voters who have been denied their right to vote in a referendum submitted to the people at a general election, pursuant to section 11 of article VII of the New York Constitution, a contention raised for the first time on appeal; the record before Special Term is bereft of this argument and is also missing the necessary supporting allegations that the individual plaintiffs were voters.

Parties -- Standing

3. In an action brought by special interest groups and individual citizens and taxpayers to permanently enjoin defendants from issuing bonds for prison construction (see Prison Construction Act, L 1983, ch 56), the individual plaintiffs have standing as taxpayers; if they are not allowed to pursue the action, it is unlikely that a judicial challenge to the act's legality can be mounted. However, the organizational plaintiffs do not have independent standing to maintain the action, absent substantiation of a specific interest or aggrieved members.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Prison Construction Act

4. The [***3] Prison Construction Act (L 1983, ch 56), which authorized the New York State Urban Development Corporation to finance and participate in the development of correctional facilities on behalf of the State Department of Correctional Services by issuing bonds and notes to finance construction, does not violate section 8 (subd 1) of article VII of the New York Constitution, which prohibits a gift or loan of the State's credit, since no indemnity has been undertaken with respect to the bonds. Further, debts, whether contingent or fixed, paid out of current appropriations in the State's general fund, replenished by the collection of taxes and which are necessary for the ongoing business of State government, are not subject to a challenge under section 11 of article VII of the State Constitution for failure to submit the question to a referendum at a general election; courts may not invalidate policy decisions of the Legislature enacted into law out of a mere preference for a different or more restrained approach.

Beare v. Byrne

After the teacher commenced the defamation action against the professor, he requested that the county provide him with representation. The county denied representation, and he retained private counsel. The court modified the order denying the professor's motion for partial summary judgment because he was clearly entitled to representation by the county in that he was acting in his official capacity.

Ekstein v. Common Council of Gloversville
